                   CREDITAND PROGRAMSUMMARY


                                      Uganda
            UGANDA POVERTY REDUCTIONSUPPORT CREDIT 7


Borrower           Republic o fUganda
ImplementingAgency The Office o f the Prime Minister (OPM) coordinates PEAP
                   implementationand the PRSC inclose cooperation with other
                   ministries.
Amount              SDR125 million (US$200 million equivalent).

Terms              IDA credit. StandardIDAterms. 40 years maturity and 10-year grace
                   period.
Tranching          Two tranches of SDR62.5 million each. The first tranche to be released
                   upon effectiveness expected June 2008 and the second tranche upon
                   completion o f tranche release triggers expected inFY2008/09. The two
                   tranche PRSC7 support for FY2007/08 and FY2008/09 i s needed in
                   order to have the next PRSC series fully conform to the Paris Principles
                   of donor harmonization and enhanced predictability for Government's
                   budget planning.
Description        This program document presents a proposed credit to The Republic o f
                   Uganda for SDR125 million (US$ 200 million equivalent), to finance
                   the Seventh Poverty Reduction Support Operation (PRSC7). Building
                   on six preceding PRSCs, PRSC7 i s the third and final operation in a
                   programmatic development policy lending series to support Uganda's
                   thirdPoverty EradicationAction Plan (PEAP) approved by the Cabinet
                   inNovember 2004:

                   PEAP Pillar I: Economic Management. The Strategic Objective i s to
                   maintain macro economic stability consistent with rapid private sector
                   led growth. The PRSC support seeks to enhance budget rationalization
                   in line with PEAP priorities and ensure efficient use of scarce
                   resources.

                   PEAP Pillar 2: Enhance Production, Competitiveness And Incomes.
                   Strategic objectives supported by the PRSC are: (i)efficient and
                   competitive private sector; (ii)increased and more efficient agricultural
                   production, including increased marketing and increased access to land
                   titling; and (iii)
                                    strengthened financial sector in support o f increased
                   production o f goods and services.

                   PEAP Pillar 4: Good Governance. Strategic objectives supported by
                   the PRSC are: (i)strengthened legal and justice systems including
                   increased efficiency in the commercial justice; (ii)transparent and
                   efficient public financial management and public procurement; (iii)
                   strengthened public sector management and accountability; (iv)
                   reduced corruption; and (v) strengthened local government systems for
                   service delivery.



                                          i

            PEAP Pillar 5: Human Development. Strategic objectives supported by
            the PRSC are: (i) educated society including increased access
                                 better
            and quality o f primary, secondary, and tertiary education; (ii)healthier
            Ugandans, including improved access, quality and utilization o f health
            care services; improved sexual and reproductive health care services
            (obstetric, adolescent, and family planning services) and control of
            major communicable diseases (immunizable diseases, tuberculosis,
            malaria, and HIV/AIDS); and (iii)improved water and sanitation
            system including Improved access to safe water supply and sanitation,
            and Improvedwater facilities maintenance.

Benefits    The proposed PRSC7 supports the economic and institutional
            environment for the sustained implementation o f PEAP reforms so as
            to reach the poverty reduction, human development, and growth targets
            laid out in that strategy. It underpins the Government's objectives by
            providing external resources necessary for the execution o f the
            Government budget, and by providing knowledge and technical advice
            on the implementation o fUganda's poverty reduction reforms.

Risks       Riskscanbe broadly classified into two categories:

            Risks that reflect the broader Ugandacontext

            (i)Policies in support of private sector development. There are
            concerns    about possible policy reversal (commitment            to the
            environment) and state interventions inproductive sectors (land and tax
            giveaways). The Bank i s engaged in proactive policy dialogue at all
            levels to prevent policy reversals and continues to               support
            strengthening o f institutions and procedures. Reforms in the land sub-
            sector are a critical factor for improving agricultural productivity.
            There i s however a risk that public controversy and sensitivity continue
            delaying reforms. (ii)Management of oil revenues. Uganda has
            recently found economically viable quantities o f high grade oil in the
            west, leading to acceleration in exploration efforts and discussion on
            revenue sharing arrangements. Management o f possible large scale oil
            revenues i s a sensitive issue for regional peace and for sharing the
            benefits internally. The government recently produced the National Oil
            and Gas Policy to ensure transparency and to prevent waste and
            :orruption in the use o f oil revenues. The Government i s working with
            Development Partners to proactively mitigate these risks and the Bank
            has offered technical advice through the Extractive Industries
            rransparency Initiative. (iii)Tackling governance issues. Although
            Zovernment has made progress in highlighting corruption involving
            ise o f public funds, the investigation and prosecution tend to be slow
            ind under-resourced. Corruption threatens public confidence, wastes
            scarce resources, and reduces the impact o f Government programs. The
            ?RSC dialogue has been broadened to tackle corruption through a
            nulti-pronged approach o f enhancing accountability (Public Financial
            Management and procurement), programming for better value for
            noney (sector reviews), and facilitating enforcement (case backlog
            nanagement, special anti-corruption court). (iv) Weak public sector


                                   ..
                                   11

                   capacity. Uganda needs to address public sector weaknesses arising
                   from inadequate incentives and weak accountability for results. A
                   comprehensive pay reform i s currently not possible due to limited
                   resources and already high share o f employee costs in the budget. The
                   Bank i s working with a Government task force on various scenarios for
                   designing a viable public sector reform which include right-sizing o f
                   civil service, implementing an agreed pay policy and cost efficiency
                   and effectiveness in the use o f human resources. In addition, the
                   Government      needs   to     strengthen  human       resource   planning,
                   performance management and enforcement o f disciplinary measures in
                   order to improve productivity and accountability for service delivery.
                   (v) Exogenous shocks, arising from adverse weather patterns or global
                   economic slowdown, can adversely affect the Ugandan economy.
                   Impatience with the current energy crisis i s giving rise to the view that
                   fiscal and monetary policies have been too conservative. The Bank has
                   been advising the Government to maintain sound macroeconomic
                   policies and to continue pursuing structural and institutional reforms.
                   The PRSC continues to support efforts to enhance production,
                   competitiveness, and incomes o f the rural poor who are the most
                   vulnerable to these shocks.

                   Risks specific to the PRSC:

                   (i)Growthof publicadministrationbudget.AsnotedinPRSC5and6
                   the growth o f the public administration budget has remained a
                   challenge for PRSC7. Expenditures in amending the constitution in
                   2005, holding national elections in 2006, and hosting the CHOGM in
                   2007 were among the factors contributing to the growth o f public
                   administration expenditures. The Bank i s closely monitoring the budget
                   and the impact o f these one o f f events on expenditures in service
                   delivery sectors as part o f its assessment o f the triggers for the release
                   o f the second tranche o f PRSC7. (ii) Financial accountability. Budget
                   discipline needs to be maintained and competing budget priorities
                   balanced through the existing consultative process. Reliance on
                   supplementary allocations needs to be limited to emergencies. Public
                   procurement often relied on provisions for waiver o f competitive
                   bidding, and compliance on established procedures generally fell. The
                   PRSC7 prior actions are focusing more on strengthening budget
                   reporting, auditing, and compliance with the procurement law. (iii)
                   Sustainability of service delivery. The demographic factors point to
                   potential problems in sustaining current programs, and thus new
                   programs will need to be considered carefully with populationpressure
                   duly taken into account. The Bank i s undertaking sectoral PERs to
                   improve efficiency and highlighting the issue o f sustainable service
                   deliverv inthe sector discussions.

                              IDA PROGRAMDOCUMENTFORA
      PROPOSEDSEVENTHPOVERTYREDUCTIONSUPPORT CREDIT (PRSC7)
                                TO THE REPUBLICOF UGANDA

                                     I. INTRODUCTION

 1.       This program document presents a proposed credit to the Republic o fUganda for
 SDR125 million or US$200 million equivalent to finance the seventh Poverty Reduction
 Support Operation (PRSC7). The proposed credit will supplement Government's own
 resources and funding from other budget support development partners during two fiscal
 years, namely FY2007/08 and FY2008/09.

 2.       The proposedPRSC7is the third andfinal annualbudgetsupport operation
 in a programmaticdevelopment policylending(DPL) series that supports Uganda's
 third PovertyEradicationAction Plan (PEAP)' launchedin May 2005 and covering
the period2005 to 2008. The implementation o f the third PEAP started against the back
drop o f continued progress in reducing the number o f households that live in poverty
(down to 31.5 percent in 2005 from 38 percent in2002). This laudable achievement was
made despite a number o f exogenous shocks that hit the economy, including a prolonged
drought that dampened agricultural production and contributed to a severe shortfall in
hydroelectric power generation. More recently, Uganda has also been facing a
significantly increased cost o f doing business on account o f the post-election violence in
Kenya. The Government has now started a process to revise the PEAP for the third time,
starting with a retrospective review o f decade-long achievements under PEAP and the
development o f a long-term strategic vision for sustained growth in Uganda. The next
multi-year PRSC series will be designed and presented to the Board once the core
elements o f the new PEAP are defined by Government. As in earlier years, it is expected
that the next PRSC series will continue to be a key component o f the Bank's support for
Uganda as spelled out inthe UgandaJoint Assistance Strategy (UJAS).'

3.        The proposed PRSC7 supports four of the five pillars of the third PEAP,
and can be grouped broadly into four areas: reforms to enhance productivity and
competitiveness (investment climate and rural development); effective delivery o f social
services (health, education, water sector programs, and decentralization); public sector
management (budget execution, public financial management, procurement, and anti-
corruption); and gender, infant and maternal mortality, and population growth. The
program document describes reforms to be supported by PRSC7 and summarizes
Government's achievements under the third PEAP.


1. The Poverty Eradication Action Plan i s Uganda's PRSP. The first PEAP was prepared in 1997 and
provided the model for the PRSP approach. I t was revised in2000 and again in2004. The third and latest
revision was based on broad consultation and took 18 months to complete. It was approved by the Cabinet
inNovember 2004 as the national framework for development. See the Joint IDA-IMFStaffAdvisoryNote,
Report No. 32698-UG, June 23,2005.
2. Uganda Joint Assistance Strategy (UJAS) i s a harmonized program o f donor support linked to the third
PEAP. UJAS was developed jointly with development partners and was presented to the Board along with
PRSCS.


                                                    1

4.       The PRSC7 reflects a broad and ambitious agenda of Government-led
reforms under the PEAP. The Government remains committed to deepening these
reforms under the new 5-year National Development Plan (successor to the third PEAP,
currently under preparation), and addressing systematically the capacity constraints that
stand inthe way o f faster and better results inmany areas. The program, including prior
actions, has been developed jointly with the Government o f Uganda (GOU) in close
cooperation with other development partners, including the African Development Bank
(AfDB), Germany, Ireland, the Netherlands, Norway, Sweden, and the UnitedKingdom
whose support i s contingent upon satisfactory PRSC progress.

5.       The overridingtheme of the PRSC7operationis to supportthe Government
in delivering better services on the ground and to create a better environment for
growth and poverty reduction. Therefore, the program focuses on improving the
capacity o f the public sector to deliver services, build public infrastructure assets,
improve accountability, and facilitate the growth o f the private sector through better
prioritization and stronger focus on results. Key challenges faced by the Government are
summarized below.

6.       Better and more transparent public sector performanceis essentialto put
Uganda on a sustainable path of rapid growth. Uganda's current savings are not
sufficient to meet the medium-terminvestment priorities, including filling a large gap in
physical infrastructure neededto promote private sector development. Identified areas for
investment are so numerous and large-scale that they need to be prioritized and spaced
over time. In this setting, using limited resources strategically and without preventable
waste i s o f central importance. Recent decline in budget support to Uganda i s an
indication that foreign aid is finite and based increasingly on demonstration o f solid
performance. The final year o f implementation under the third PEAP therefore presents
an opportunity to take stock and re-focus the program for better results.

7.       The execution of the budget was complicatedby new initiatives, significant
reallocation of budgetary resources to the Commonwealth Heads of Government
Meeting (CHOGM), and the energy crisis during FY2007/08. The current energy
crisis is giving rise to the view that fiscal and monetary policies have been too
conservative and constraining resources needed for infrastructure investments. The post-
election violence in Kenya, disrupting landlocked Uganda's gateway to the coast has put
in question the country's over-dependency on a single transport route, underscoring the
need to invest in alternative routes. On balance, the Government has been able to resist
the temptation o f meddlingdirectly inbusiness development, but faces an equally strong
demandfrom businessesfor a variety o fincentives.

8.       The orientation of the budget toward achieving priority outcomes of
deliveringbetter services and upgradinginfrastructurecan be more easily achieved
if some parts of the Government refrain from spending beyondapproved budgets.
Recurring overruns in public administration and security sector budgets constrain the
needed expansion o f services and investment, and often take away resources mid-year
from ongoing programs. During FY2006/07 and FY2007/08, this problem was
compounded by the resources allocated to prepare for CHOGM hosted by Uganda.


                                             2

9.       The private sector in Uganda has been very successful at finding
opportunities, with a respectable level of product discovery and development of
export markets. The Government's support provided to the private sector and rural
farmers needs to be in the form o f removing obstacles, creating an environment that
makes it cheaper and easier to tap market opportunities and adopt better technology to
improve productivity and quality of their output. It should not be inthe form o f measures
that temporarily alter the prices (e.g. through subsidies) as they do not improve the
competitiveness o f Ugandan businesses in the long-run. The rural development and
competitiveness agenda under PRSC7 will aim to support Government reforms that
benefit the broader community o f Ugandan businesses and farmers and improve the
investment climate.

10.      There is a renewed commitment to fight corruption, but investigationand
prosecution in recent cases have been painfully slow and under-resourced. The
PRSC7 will assess the adequacy o f the reforms and objectives achieved thus far, based on
recent and ongoing analytic work such as the 2007 Public Expenditure Review and
Country Procurement Assessment Report. Most importantly, these assessments will
enable a more concerted approach to reducing corruption and mismanagement o f public
resources at the source, in tandem with existing laws and policies to combat corruption
through enforcement when it happens.

11.      Prior actions for key service social sectors under PRSCshavebeen linkedto
the annual sector reviews, but the Government is lookingfor ways to link resource
allocation to better prioritized objectives and clearly defined results. The sector
review process i s built upon a series o f technical reviews, value for money and design
studies for sub-sector issues, consultations for drafting sector budgets, as well as surveys
and management information systems to gauge progress regularly. Based on these inputs,
the annual Sector Reviews assess the performance for the previous year, identifygaps and
decide on priorities for the following year. Some sectors (e.g., rural development and
public sector management) have chosen to focus PRSC prior actions on a subset o f sector
undertakings, and to undertake additional analytical work to bring new issues and
evidence to sector discussions (public expenditure reviews, tracking studies, procurement
audits). Rapid population growth (quadrupling in the next 50 years) adds the dimension
o f sustainability to the challenges faced by the sectors. Given that the resources cannot
grow indefinitely, there are bound to be tradeoffs between the access (e.g., enrollment)
and quality (e.g., education outcomes), and the issue o f achieving more with less
(efficiency) was a key topic in2007 sector reviews.




                                             3

                                   11.     COUNTRY CONTEXT

 POVERTY, INEQUALITY, AND QUALITYOF LIFE INDICATORS

 12.        The policy reforms undertaken since the 1990s have yielded robust
 economic growthand significantpovertyreductionin Uganda.The 2005/06 National
 Household Survey showed that the poverty rate declined to 31 percent, down from 38
 percent in 2002/03 and 56 percent in 1992/93. Growth in household income from
 improved distribution and better crop prices (particularly coffee), has contributed to the
 decline inpoverty and inequality, especially between 2002/03 and 2005/06. However, the
 ruraVurban and inter-regional disparities inpoverty rates as well as trends still exist, with
 the rural areas and North-East regions doing relatively worse. Northern Uganda, which
 has endured prolonged insecurity, has the highest rate o fpoverty at 61 percent. Consistent
 with the decline inpoverty in2005/06, inequality declined-the               Ginicoefficient fell from
 0.43 in 2002/03 to 0.41 in 2005/06.3 However, further deliberate government policy
 actions are needed ifinequality is to be brought down to the 1992/93 levels.

 13.        Poverty reduction in both rural and urban areas has been highest among
 households employed in the formal sector and off-farm activities. Compared to the
 decline o f 23 percent in the national poverty level between 2002/03 and 2005/06, the
 poverty headcount for households whose head works in agriculture fell by a mere 13
 percent, as agricultural output stagnated. By 2005/06, households with a non-farm
 enterprise were, on average, 70 percent better off than those in agriculture. Households
 whose head had a wage and salary job were much less likely to be in poverty.
 Nonetheless, the asset and living conditions picture i s somewhat mixed. The poorest
 quintile in rural areas (mostly engaged in agriculture) seems to have accumulated more
 assets between 2002/03 and 2005/06, whereas the conditions for the poorest quintile in
urban areas worsened on several aspects, particularly with respect to housing conditions
 and access to sanitatiodelectricity. The focus of the 2004 PEAP on accelerated growth
 and enhanced income generation among rural households i s gradually being reflected in
the expenditure framework, which seeks to shift resources toward infrastructure and
activities intended to raise farm productivity in rural areas. With increasing budgetary
pressures, the key service delivery sectors (health, education, and water and sanitation)
are being challenged to create fiscal space through measures that enhance efficiency and
value for money. The service delivery sectors contributed to progress made over the last
decade inpoverty reduction, and thus the resource constraints they face inthe short-term
will require attentionto prevent adverse effects on the poverty front.

 14.       Results of the 2006 Uganda Demographic and Health Survey (DHS) show
some improvementsin access to health care services and quality of life indicators.
Total fertility declined slightly to 6.7 children in 2006 from 6.9 children per woman of


3. Uganda's level o f inequality now stands at about average for Sub-Saharan Africa non-mineral exporting
economies. The Gini coefficient i s the standard measure o f inequality, which considers the distribution o f
national income among the population. By ranking the income measure and population on a scale o f 1 to
100, the coeficient shows the proportion of the populationreceiving a given share o f income, i.e., ifperfect
income equality existed, then say 20 percent o f the richest (or poorest) population should be getting 20
percent o f national income.


                                                     4

 child-bearing age in 2001. However, fertility remains high inrural areas and among poor
 and less-educated womena4High fertility rates and the resultinghigh population growth
 (3.2 percent for the period 1991-2002) have adverse implications for per-capita income
 growth and poverty reduction efforts. Addressing factors leading to early motherhood, for
 example, by empowering girls through more education and delaying the age o f marriage
 are important. In addition, improving the incomes o f rural women will help to further
 lower fertility rates inUganda. Increasing access to family planningservices will not only
 help to reduce fertility rates, but also improve maternal and child health. Currently,
 contraceptive prevalence i s only about 24 percent, and about 41 percent o f married
 women who needed familyplanningare not served.

 15.      The DHS 2006 reportsimprovedaccess to anti-natalcare andvaccinationof
 children, which substantially improved the health of both children and mothers.
 About 44 percent o f children aged 12 to 23 months were fully immunized in 2006
 compared to only 37 percent in 2000/01. Infant mortality declined to 77 per 1,000 live
births from 88 per 1,000 in 2001. Maternal mortality also declined to 435 per 100,000
women givingbirthfrom 505 per 100,000 in2001,

 16.      However, exposure to malaria-transmitting mosquitoes remains high
because of low usage of treatedmosquito nets. In2006, only 34 percent o fhouseholds
owned a mosquito net, of which only 16 percent were treated with insecticide. On the
night o f the 2006 survey, only 22 percent o f children and 24 percent o fpregnant women
slept under a mosquito net.

RECENT ECONOMIC DEVELOPMENTS

 17.      Uganda's prudent macroeconomic policies continue to generate robust growth
rates. During the 10 years to 2005/06 -representing a full decade after the coffee price
boom period - GDP growth averaged 5.5 percent per annum. This was in spite o f the
volatility in agricultural output arising from prolonged drought conditions in many parts
o f the country; constraints to industrial production from the energy crisis; and the adverse
impact on all sectors from highand volatile world oil prices. In2006/07, GDP growth is
estimated to have recovered to 6.5 percent, especially as result o f a rebound in
agricultural production from improved weather, but also supported by strong regional
demandfor Uganda's goods and services. The outlook for 2007/08 and2009/10 envisions
average real growth o f about 7.0 percent, boosted by stepped-up investment in
infrastructure.

18.       Inthe short term, growthreboundisexpected oncepeacereturns to theNorthon
a sustainable basis. However, exogenous shocks (such as oil price surges and disruption
o f transport routes), internal budgetarypressures, high population growth, and failure to
address infrastructure constraints in a timely manner are major risks for faster growth.
First, volatility inagriculture production continues to drive the trend o f GDP-improved

               ~~

weather during 2006/07 i s estimated to have restored agricultural growth to 4.2 percent
compared to a decline o f 0.2 percent recorded in2005/06. Second, while the power crisis
subsided with the installation o f additional thermal supply, permanent relief and the cost
o fproduction are not expected untilBujagali Hydro power station becomes operational in
2010/1l.' Third, the rise ininternational oil prices has been a major shock for Uganda as
a net importerY6with the average spot price o f US$ 61 per barrel in 2006/07 (higher than
the levels observed in 2003/04 by 97 percent). Oil prices have continued to increase over
the first seven months o f 2007/08, surpassing US$lOO per barrel, introducing an
additional element o f risk to a country that now relies heavily on diesel-powered
electricity generation. Fourth, the events during the post-election violence in Kenya
brought to the fore the risk o f landlocked Uganda's over-reliance on a single transport
route to the coast. The temporary closure o f railway operations between Kampala and
Mombasa, disruption o f road freight transport, shortage o f transport fuel, and subsequent
increase infuel prices by over 20 percent would all have been detrimental to growth ifthe
situation had lasted longer.

 19.      There i s increasing hope for a negotiated peace inthe North following a cessation
o f hostilities in August 2006 and steady progress in the negotiations between the GOU
and the Lord's Resistance Army (LRA) in Juba (south Sudan). As a result, the terms o f
the peace agreement have been worked out and await signing. If these talks lead to a
durable peace and demobilization o f the LRA, the potential for a surge in economic
production i s highgiventhat the conflict-affected areas are among the most fertile regions
o f Uganda. In fact, large numbers o f internally displaced people (IDPs) have already
returnedto their homes and resumed farming, especially inthe Lango and Teso regions.
However, as outlined in the Government's Peace, Recovery and Development Plan
(PRDP) for Northern Uganda, there are also very significant financing needs for
resettlement, rehabilitationo f social and physical infrastructure, andprovision o f services.
Most o fthe additional costs for reconstruction are foreseen to be supported through donor
funding.

20.       Over the long term, sustaining growth at higher levels requires addressing the
most binding constraints, currently manifested as infrastructure gaps (mainly in the
energy and transport sectors), but also others that may arise on account o f weak financial
intermediation, skills gaps, and high population growth. First, "fiscal space" for public
infrastructure investments needs to be found through increased efficiency and value for
money, aid financing, better tax revenue performance,' and through concessional
borrowing. Second, the Government needs to strengthen financial intermediation and
propagate a skills-based curriculum development program to help close education and
skills gaps and reduce unemployment. Third, the highpopulation growth rate is not only

6. While prospects for oil extraction within Uganda are promising, the level o f oil reserves has yet to be

MACROECONOMIC                            ENVIRONMENT AND DEBT SUSTAINABILITY

21.           The macroeconomic environmentremainslargelystable, in spite of the risks
posed by the exogenous shocks mentionedabove. Inflationary pressures from adverse
weather conditions, power shortages, and energy price shocks that raised domestic
inflation above the target of 5 percent for three consecutive years had started to subside
during the first half o f 2007/08. However, upward pressure on prices through increased
freight charges following the post-election violence in Kenya and higher oil prices have



                                                                           7

pushed inflation to 6.5 percent by January 2008, from an average o f 5.3 percent during
the first half o f 2007/08. The foreign exchange market felt pressure to appreciate,
especially at the end o f 2006/07, due to increasedportfolio flows, regional export demand
 for Ugandan goods, and expectations about Uganda's oil prospects. The average value o f
the shilling appreciated against the US dollar by 3 percent in 2006/07. The real effective
exchange rate (REER) also appreciated by about 2 percent in 2006/07. During the first
halfo f 2007/08, the shilling appreciated against the dollar by about 2.1 percent. Uganda's
terms o f trade continued to improve due to the sustained rebound in export prices during
2005/06 and 2006/07; however, with increasing oil prices and expected lower global
demand, the terms o f trade are expected to worsen in the medium term. The external
position also improved, also supported by grant disbursements o f 6.0 percent o f GDP,
compared to 5.8 percent realized in 2005/06. International reserves also increased to 6.5
months o f import cover in 2006/07 as market conditions constrained the Bank o f
Uganda's sale o f forex to manage liquidity.

22.      The financial sector is sound and stable, but needs to generate higher
domestic savings andlendingandto address the gaps inlong-termfinanceand rural
financial services. Gross national savings increased from 13.2 percent o f GDP in
2005/06 to 15.8 percent in2006/07 and credit to the private sector edged to 9.0 percent o f
GDP, but remained well below the Sub-Saharan Africa average o f 17 percent. Capital
market financing, in particular for long term investment, i s limited and the pension
system requires urgent reform. The pension reform process which had stalled for a long
time has recently been rekindled. Government has adopted a stepwise approach for the
reform by first puttinginplace a regulatory framework, which will then form the basis for
gradual liberalizationand reform o fthe sector.

23.      The Government has maintained a prudent fiscal management regime by
gradually improving revenue and restraining expansion in government
expenditures. Ongoing reforms at the Uganda Revenue Authority (URA) improved
revenue collection to 13.4 percent o f GDP in 2006/07 or a full percentage point higher
than the collection during the pre-reform period. The fiscal deficit (excluding grants) is
estimated to have increased from 7.4 percent in 2006/07 to 8.6 percent as a result o f
increased expenditures to address the energy crisis. Concerns remain about overruns in
public administration expenditures and some accumulation o f arrears toward the end o f
financial years. The energy crisis has also had a significant fiscal impact, with the
Government allocating about Ush 100 billion to the energy fund in 2005/06 and an
additional Ush210 billion in2006/07.

24.     To finance the increasing fiscal deficit, net foreign financing more than doubled
to 3.7 percent o f GDP, compared to 1.5 percent in2005/06. This level o f financing i s still
lower than the 4.6 percent in 2000/01, when the deficit was at its peak o f 5.3 percent o f
GDP. Incontrast, domestic financing has been declining as the Government increases its
savings with the bankingsystem. The domestic deficit, which i s the fiscal aggregate with
the largest impact on domestic liquidity creation, increased from 2.8 percent to 3.5
percent in2006/07, but is much lower than 6.5 percent recorded in2001/02. However, the
Bank o f Uganda's strategy o f increasingly usingthe sale o f foreign exchange to manage
liquidity has mitigated the impact o f the deficit on domestic interest rates over the past


                                              8

 three years. Private sector credit was also robust, at above 20 percent in 2005/06 and
 2006/07; analysis continues to show that structural factors are more important than
 interest rates in Uganda's credit market. When combined with grants, total aid flows into
 Uganda o f about 10percent are much higherrelative to the country's financial aggregates,
 hence the cost o f monetary policy o f about 1 percent o f GDP (compared to about 0.5
 percent for other countries in the region), arising from the low level o f development o f
 the financial and foreign exchange markets.

 25.      External debt sustainability. Uganda's risk o f debt distress has been greatly
 reduced through the Multilateral Debt Reduction Initiative (MDRI) which cancelled 100
 percent o f eligible debts to the IMF, ADF, and IDA. A recent Debt Sustainability
 Analysis* confirms that Uganda i s at low risk o f debt distress thanks to the favorable
 performance o f the external sector, government focus on fiscal consolidation, and
 maintenance o f a prudent debt policy. The MDRIreduced the net present value (NPV) o f
 the external debt-to-exports ratio from a pre-MDRIlevel o f 229 percent in 2004/05 to 46
 percent in 2005/06, and fbrther to 37.8 percent in 2006/07. The reduced risk o f debt
 distress notwithstanding, prudent debt management and an efficient allocation o f
resources will still be critical to maintaining debt at sustainable levels over the long term.
 To this end, the Government has prepared a new Debt Strategy inDecember 2007.


                           111.    THE GOVERNMENT PROGRAM

26.       The Government's program is contained in the third Poverty Eradication
Action Plan (PEAP) approved in November 2004.9It restates Uganda's ambition to
become a middle income country within 20 years, and sets out targets for key "strategic
results" in income poverty and inequality, improved human development, and increased
GDP growth.

27.       The nationalbudgetdevotes a large share of resources toward achievingthe
strategic objectivesset forth in the PEAP. Since adoption o f the Poverty Action Fund
(PAF) in 1997, which operates as a virtual, ring-fenced find to ensure spending on
activities with a high poverty reduction impact, a progressively increasing share o f the
budget has been allocated to PEAP priorities. Although PAF shares have remained at a
respectable level (34 percent o f discretionary budget), recent spending pressures arising
from the power crisis and the hosting o f the 2007 CHOGM have necessitated the
Government to shift a significant amount o f resources away from key service delivery
sectors.

28.      An annualized policy action matrix for the third PEAP is used to monitor
achievement of measurabletargets andprogresstoward agreedPEAP outcomes. Bi-
annual Poverty Status Reports and the Annual PEAP Implementation Reviews (APIR)"

prioritization, work planning, performance assessments, and development partner
dialogue. In addition to these reports, the PRSC7 will continue to rely on sector
performance reports and the available surveydata (UNHS 2005/06 and DHS 2006).

29.        A national survey program supplies good-quality data required for monitoring
and evaluation, but there is a need to adjust the frequency and timing o f the national
surveys, which do not coincide with the reviews mentioned above. A reduced sample
survey form that collects data on an annual basis would allow monitoring and evaluation
in real-time and could potentially allow national surveys to be conducted at longer
intervals (e.g. tri-annual household survey, bi-annual national integrity survey, tri-annual
service delivery survey, etc.).

30.        The current PEAPcovers the period2005 to 2008, thus the Governmenthas
recently initiated the process of revising this plan into a 5-year National
DevelopmentPlan (NDP). This effort is beingledby a steering committee composed of
the Office o f the Prime Minister (OPM), Ministry o f Finance, Planning and Economic
Development (MoFPED) and the National Planning Authority (NPA). The Bank's
analytical work program will be geared to informing the drafting o f the NDP, buildingon
a recently completed Country Economic Memorandum, Diagnostic Trade Integrated
Study, and 2007 Public Expenditure Review, among others. The potential areas for a
continued policy dialogue are summarized below, but will be revisited once the NDP i s
completed:

    0     Service delivery. Expand access to education, health, and water and sanitation
          services, with concurrent improvements in the quality o f services. Sectors will
          need to adopt a much stronger focus on outcomes, cost effectiveness, and
          sustainability o f service delivery. The enhanced linkage between the budget and
          outcomes, and the empowerment o f local governments in providing and
          monitoring deliveryo f services will be emphasized.

    0     Governance and accountability. Enhance accountability in public finance and

          the Government's financial and procurement practices, and better oversight and
          prosecution o f financial and procurement irregularities. Open public access to
          information on the above. Prosecute and take administrative action against abuse
          o f power, corruption, and waste o f public funds. Support anti-corruption
          authorities, the anticipated Special Anti-Corruption Court, and the Judiciary.

    0     Improve functioning of the public sector. Improve the capacity of the Public
          Service to deliver better results at a reasonable cost. Through better human
         resource planning, rationalization and restructuring o f public service institutions,
          implementation o f public sector pension reforms, enhancement o f pay and
         introduction o f a comprehensive performance management system, the Public

        growth and poverty reduction. Strict prioritization within the existing Public

    0   Sustain high pro-poor growth. The Government aims to bring about broad-

          IV.     BANK SUPPORTFORTHE GOVERNMENT STRATEGY

LINKTOUGANDA        JOINT ASSISTANCE   STRATEGY (UJAS)
31.       The Uganda Joint Assistance Strategy (UJAS), discussed by the Board in
January 2006, is the country assistance strategy to support implementationof the
third PEAP and achieve the Millennium Development Goals. Further objectives
include strong collaboration and harmonization among development partners andwith the
Government, as well as a stronger focus on results and outcomes. As part o f the
harmonization agendaunder the UJAS, an exercise to bring about an effective division o f
labor among development partners has been launched.

COLLABORATIONWITH THE IMFAND OTHERDONORS
32.      The Government's macroeconomic program is supported by a new three-
year Policy Support Instrument (PSI) approved by the IMF Board in December
2006 (see Annex 4). The second review under this PSI was successfully concluded in
November 2007 with all assessment criteria met except the one onbase money which was
allowed to temporarily grow faster to curb foreign exchange market instability. The
review highlightedthe importance o f addressing the country's infrastructure deficit and
the need to ensure that oil resources are used prudently and transparently. In addition,
controlling non-priority spending, strengthening public expenditure management,
deepening financial sector development, and improving the business environment were
also deemed critical. These recommendations are fully consistent with those highlighted
in PRSCs as well as in the Bank's economic and sector work. IMF concentrates on
macroeconomic and financial sector issues, while social sectors and structural and
governance reforms are supported by the PRSC.

33.      The Bank has been working with developmentpartnersengaged in general
budget support to establisha Joint BudgetSupport Operation (JBSO) anchored on
the PEAP. The Bank is considering the merits of participating in the JBSO in the next
PRSC series, and has provided active support in preparing the first interim Joint
Assessment Framework (JAF) under the JBSO. Box 1summarizes progress inUganda on
implementing the aid harmonization agenda and good practices on conditionality. As




                                            11

 development partners already participate actively inPRSC discussions", they have opted
 to include PRSC7 prior actions in the interim JAF, in addition to the quantitative
 indicatorsjointly agreed among development partners. The timetable has been set to align
 with the government's own planning,budgetingandoversight cycle, including the annual
 sector reviews that take place from October to December. Bearing in mind that JAF
 largely draws from the PEAP Policy and Results Matrix, the ongoing drafting o f a 5-year
 National Development Plan will require that the content o f the JAF remain fluid and
 subject to revisions in line with the priorities agreed annually during sector reviews. The
 working assumption i s that the interim JAF assessment undertaken by the JBS
 development partners in January 2008 would enable the donors to provide indicative
 commitments for bilateral budget support for FY2008/09 as the Government drafts the
 National Budget FrameworkPaper.l2

 34.      Inline with the objective of improving predictability of aid, particularly general
 budget support, the PRSC7 proposes to provide funding for two financial years, namely
 FY2007/08 and FY2008/09. This option allows the Bank to have the next PRSC series to
 fully conform to the Paris Principles of donor harmonization and enhanced predictability
 for Government's budget planning. (see paragraphs 44-45).

LESSONSLEARNED
36.      The stocktaking report for PRSCs 1-3 concluded that the PRSC was perceived to
be the single best vehicle for helping Uganda advance its system-wide reform process,
and for making progress on service delivery and crosscutting issues. It also identified
challenges for the instrument, including the need to (i)  focus more on income generation;
(ii) agraduatedresponse,especiallyinthecaseofreformsbeingoff-track; (iii)
    clarify
avoid being overloaded; (iv) take into account the weak capacity o f local governments;
and (v) identify and measure results. Many o f these challenges still remained during
preparation o f PRSC7, and in some cases, exacerbated by inadequate fimding (e.g.
decentralization o f service delivery through local governments) and emergence o f new
parallel initiatives (e.g. Prosperity For All, and Rural Development Strategy). Recent
Implementation completion reports (ICRs) for the first series o f PRSCs (1-3) and the
stand-alone PRSC4 also highlighted issues o f absorption capacity and fiscal
sustainability, a tendency for PRSCs to set ambitious long-term goals, and the need to
assist the GOU in developing a long-term financing strategy. To take better account o f
implementation capacity, each prior action under PRSCS through PRSC7 included a
detailed description o f the rationale, content, and policy expectations, clarifying the joint



                                             13

expectations among all actors including what the actions entail and what they will
achieve. A fiscal sustainability analysis included in the 2007 Public Expenditure Review
highlighted the need for sustained investment in growth-enhancing                                physical
infrastructure, and for sectors to make better use o f existing resources and to plan for
service delivery to a growing population.

   Box 1. Good PracticePrincipleson Conditionality

   Principle 1-Reinforceownership
   Uganda has been a pioneer in country ownership of its development strategy. Uganda's first Poverty
   Eradication Action Plan (PEAP) in 1997 provided the model for PRSPs worldwide, and it has since
  been revised twice. The current Government program is contained in the third PEAP, which was
   approved by the Cabinet in November 2004 following broad consultations. The PEAP has been
   instrumental in shaping sector-wide approaches to investment and reform, and the Bank has played a
   supporting role in this process, including via its analytical work. The Country Economic
  Memorandum, for example, i s helping the Government identify priority policy actions to facilitate
   accelerated growth, given the greater emphasis on growth inthe revised PEAP. The Government is
   also using the findings o f the Bank's 2007 Public Expenditure Review to formulate the budget for
  FY2008109.

  Principle 2 -Agree in advancewith the Government and other financial partnerson a
  coordinated accountabilityframework
  Donor harmonization and alignment with Government priorities is advanced in Uganda. The
  Government developed an annualized policy action matrix for the third PEAP and requested that
  development partners use the PEAP matrix and the findings o f the Annual PEAP Implementation
  Review (APIR) to determine their assistance. Budget support donors, including the Bank, are in the
  process o f developing a Joint Budget Support Operation (JBSO) anchored on the PEAP, which would
  essentially formalize andbuildon the existing collaborative arrangement under PRSC.

  Principle3 -Customizethe accountabilityframework and modalitiesof Banksupportto
  country circumstances
  Policies supported by the PRSC represent actions that the Government believes will lead to the
  desired PEAP outcomes, and thus reflect country preferences. They are selectedfrom the larger PEAP
  policy matrix by the Government in consultation with donors, including the Bank. The stocktaking
  report for Uganda PRSCs 1, 2, and 3 concluded that the PRSC is the single best vehicle for helping
  Uganda advance its system-wide reform process, and for making progress on service delivery and
  crosscutting issues. The Government has said that it would like PRSC operations to be structured so
  that financing is approved before the national budget is submitted to Parliament. The Bankmay need
  to adjust the PRSC timetable to further enhance h d i n g predictability for budgeting purposes and to
  feed PRSC discussions into the Joint Assessment Framework under the proposed JBS mechanism.
  The provision of financing under PRSC7 intwo equal tranchesto support FY2007108 andFY2008109
  budgets will enable the Bank to filly conform to the Paris Principles o f donor harmonization and
  enhancedpredictability for Government's budget planning inthe next PRSC series.

  Principle4 -Choose only actions criticalto achieve results as conditionsfor disbursement
  Prior actions for PRSC7 are based on those envisioned at the beginning o f the three-year
  programmatic cycle of budget support @e., under PRSCS). The PRSC triggers represent actions and
  outputs that are considered critical by the Government to the success o f its reforms. Several o f the
  PRSC prior actions are basedon satisfactory completion o f undertakings agreedto inexisting sectoral
  review processes (e.g., ineducation), thus avoiding creation o f new and overlapping review structures
  and indicators. However, the Bank continues to provide important input to the sector review process
  to improve prioritization and outcome-orientation.




                                                   14

  1Box 1. GoodPracticePrincipleson Conditionality (cont'd)

   Principle5 -Conducttransparentprogress reviewsconduciveto predictable and
   performance-basedfinancial support
   The PEAP policy matrix served as the basis for the first APIR, conducted in February 2007, and
   guided policy analysis, budget prioritization, work planning, performance assessment, and
   development partner dialogue. Given the ongoing P E N evaluation there will1 be no new APIR this
   year and the forthcoming National DevelopmentPlan (NDP) is set to provide a revised set of policy
   targets to guide development partners intheir assistance to Uganda. The new Joint Budget Support
   Operation that is being developed by the Bank and development partners, including a Joint
   Assessment Framework, is anchoredon the PEAP and will be adjusted based on priorities identified
   inthe NDP.



 37.      World Bank global stocktaking of PRSCs. A global stocktaking o f PRSCs
 (2005)identified four objectives: flexibility, donor harmonization, resource predictability,
 andincreased country ownership. The PRSCs also signal the Bank's support for countries
with a proven track record, strong financial management, and robust commitment and
capacity to undertake structural and social reforms. Inmany ways Uganda's PRSC series
has been the model for PRSCs inother countries, having embraced these objectives early
on and designed a comprehensive approach that encompasses a large number o f sectors
and a wide range o f stakeholders.

ANALYTICALUNDERPINNINGS
38.       The Country Economic Memorandum (CEM) recommends a prioritized, time-
bound, and consistent set o f policy actions, investments, and interventions to remove
likely obstacles to growth and structural transformation. It has also helped refocus Bank
and donor operations and policy dialogue toward supporting economic growth. Under the
Country Economic Memorandum (CEM), the Bank has developed a SAM-based CGE
model called Maquette for MDGs Simulations (MAMS) as a tool to simulate the impact
o f specific investments, policies, and various economic and price shocks on growth and
the likely achievement o f the MDGs. This model could provide a useful tool for PSIA
work in the future. Further on the macro side, a Debt Sustainability Analysis was
performedjointly bythe Bank andFundin2007 andthe Government o f Uganda External
Debt Strategy has been prepared in December 2007. To ensure debt sustainability, the
strategy lays out a comprehensive approach to debt contracting andmanagement.

39.      The 2007 Public Expenditure Review (PER) focused on developing a fiscal
policy for growth -how to increase spending on infrastructure needed to sustain growth
inthe face o f increasing signs o f stress from expenditure pressures and fiscal tightening.
The report uses the MAMS model to assess an optimal fiscal strategy for growth in
Uganda, applies a fiscal forecasting model to recommend how to create space in the
budget for infrastructure investments and how to improve efficiency in infrastructure
spending. Significantly, the report also presents a methodology for analyzing waste and
inefficiency inthe education sector. A major findingwas that teacher absenteeism andthe
low proportion o f teacher time spent in classrooms are the biggest drivers o f inefficiency
inprimary education. This analysis underpinsthe PRSC emphasis on deepening sector



                                                  15

 dialogue on value for money inservice delivery, while also promoting efficient allocation
 to address constraints to production, competitiveness, and incomes.

 40.         The Bank completed a Diagnostic Trade Integration Study (DTIS) in 2005/06
 under the auspices o f the Integrated Framework for Trade Development inLDCs (IF)and
 incollaboration with the MinistryofTourism, Trade andIndustry. A prioritizedmatrix o f
 the DTIS recommendations approved by the Cabinet in 2007 underscores the need to
 alleviate infrastructureconstraints for land-locked Uganda, and has significantly informed
 the competitiveness and investment climate strategy. The Doing Business Survey
 continues to highlight trends in key indicators o f the business environment. Areas o f
 attention in the financial sector have been informed by the Financial Sector Assessment
 Update o f 2005 and the CEM, while the study "Reform options for public service pension
 fund in Uganda" has underpinned dialogue on public sector pension reform. A Gender
 and Growth Assessment (GGA) was completed in 2006 and identified specific policy
recommendations to strengthen women's economic empowerment; a GGA Coalition
 (comprised o f civil society, public, and private organizations) has since been formed to
 advocate implementation o f these recommendations. Support for PEAP Pillar 4 i s
informed by the Public Financial Management Performance Report and Update o f the
CIFA Action Plan (July 2006). Several studies undertaken and ongoing on ed~cation,'~
health,14 and water and sanitation" inform the human development component o f PRSC
support.


  V. THE PROPOSEDSEVENTHPOVERTY REDUCTIONSUPPORTCREDIT

OPERATIONDESCRIPTION

41.         PRSC7 i s the third and final operation in a planned series o f three PRSCs to
support implementation o f Uganda's third Poverty Eradication Action Plan (PEAP). The
proposed annual cycle o f PRSCs i s in line with the UJAS. The PRSC7 credit will
complement government and other Development Partner resources in financing priority
programs for poverty reduction and growth duringFY2007/08 and FY2008/09.

42.         Performance since PRSC6 approval. Overall macroeconomic management
remained sound in spite o f the ongoing power crisis, exchange rate fluctuations causedby
sizable short-term capital inflows, and front-loaded execution o f the budget to cater for
preparations for the CHOGM. The Government has also continued to press forward many
o fthe activities from PRSC6 inthe areas o fpublic financial management, anti-corruption,
decentralization, and public service reform. New initiatives and studies to improve the
efficiency o f public expenditures were also undertaken, such as the expenditure review in
education, value-for-money studies in the health and water sectors, and review o f PAF
expenditures. There i s a stronger interest in orienting the budget process for clearer

encouraging signs that the anti-corruption authorities are maintaining their earlier focus
on investigations and enforcement, including a comprehensive cleanup o fthe government
payroll.

43.       The achievement of some of the PRSC7 prior actions has been constrained
by the demands arising from hosting the 2007 CHOGM. As summarized in Table 2,
several areas o f reform included actions which were delayed or partially achieved.
Although the number o f PRSC7 prior actions andthe associated timetable may have been
overly ambitious, they were very useful in setting the path o f reforms in each area. As
specified inthe Letter o f Development Policy, the Government i s committed to continue
pursuingthesereforms despite the delay insome areas.

 PRSC7FINANCING ALIGNMENT TO BUDGETPROCESS
                      AND

44.       The current PRSC series (PRSC5-7) has been designedas annual budget support
operations, and thus far have disbursed funds retroactively at the end o f each financial
year to finance spendingthat had already taken place. The Government is encouraging the
development partners to improve the predictability o f their funding commitments. Inline
with this objective, it is proposed that PRSC7 fundingbe increasedto US$200 million, to
be provided in two separate tranches o f SDR62.5 million (US$lOO million equivalent)
each for FY2007/08 and FY2008/09, respectively. The proposed option would not only
facilitate ongoing budget planningfor FY2008/09, but also improve the predictability o f
budgetary resources provided by PRSC inthe future. It would also align the PRSC better
with Joint Budget Support Operations o f bilateral development partners who are in the
process o f deciding the level o f their support for FY2008/09. The first tranche would be
disbursed upon effectiveness o f the credit, whereas the second tranche would be
disbursed once the tranche release triggers outlined below are satisfactorily fulfilled. This
proposal takes into account the prior actions that have not been fully achieved by
reducing IDA'Sbudget support to Uganda from US$l50 million to US$lOO million per
year. PRSC7 financing would be supplemented by IDA'SMDRI debt relief which i s
estimated to be around US$ 55 million per year inFY2007/08 and FY2008/09.

45.       The Government is committed to fulfilling the prior actions under PRSC7 which
were delayed due to insufficient implementation capacity and owing to the reallocation o f
government resources to hosting the CHOGM in 2007. Indeed, many o f the reforms that
require legislative approval have resumed and are expected to be completed early in
FY2008/09. The second tranche financing under PRSC7 i s linked to the following
triggers: (i) maintenance o f a stable macroeconomic environment; (ii)       approval o f the
budget for FY2008/09 which is consistent with the Government's PEAP and medium-
term growth objectives; and (iii)  submission to Cabinet o f draft amendments to the PPDA
Act and Regulations. These triggers are expected to be completedby September 30,2008.

 RESULTS    FRAMEWORK
46.       The PRSC uses a subset of the PEAP policy and outcomes matrix as its
resultsframework. Outcome indicators have been selected from the full PEAP matrix to
monitor progress on specific areas supported by the series o f PRSC prior actions in each
sector. These indicators, with baseline data, progress to date, and targets for the end o f the
PEAP implementation period, are shown inTable 3 below. A more detailed presentation
o f the linkages between these indicators and the prior actions under the current PRSC
series can be seen inAnnex 2. As the current PEAP was intendedto be implemented over
the period FY2004/05-FY2007/08, the targets beyond this period have not yet been set.
Ongoing drafting o f the successor National Development Plan will establish future
targets.

 POLICYAREAS
                         PEAPPILLAR 1-ECONOMIC                MANAGEMENT
 PEAP Strategic Objective 1.0 - Macroeconomic stability consistent with rapid
 privatesector ledgrowth

 47.       Uganda's budget formulation and execution process remains transparent
 and inclusive, involving the Executive, Parliament, local governments, civil society
 organizations, and development partners. The Consultative Budget Workshops are held
 each year, leading to a National Budget Framework Paper in April.16 This budget
 formulation process is coupled with oversight o f budget execution by the Parliamentary
 Public Accounts Committees to hold spending units accountable for resources allocated
 to them. Information on how Government utilizes budgetary resources i s disseminated
 regularly and usedby the media andcivil society.

 48.       PRSCsupportedmedium-termoutcome 1.1.1: Reduce public expenditureas
 a percentageof GDPthroughbudgetrationalization.The prior action seeks to (i)                           limit
 excessive growth o f Government's overall expenditure to achieve a medium-term fiscal
 deficit target o f 6.5 percent o f GDP; (ii)   maintain poverty focus o f public expenditure by
 ensuring that Poverty Action Fund (PAF) expenditures perform at 95 percent or more;
 and (iii) growth o f expenditures bythe security and the public administration sectors
           limit
 so that increasingly larger share o f the budget can be directed to priority spending.
 Starting inFY2006/07, these objectives were to be assessed usingactual expenditure data
 for central government spending units. Under PRSCs 5 and 6, the Government has met
 the first two undertakings,but overran the budget for public administration.

 49.       Based on the FY2006/07 Budget Performance Report, both overall
 expenditure and revenues performed above projections. Cash releases (GOU
resources only) amounted to Ush3,267 billion against the approved budget o f Ush3,067
billion. Actual expenditures amounted to Ush3,254 billion or 106.1 percent o f the
 approved budget. A supplementary appropriation o f Ush310 billion, o f which Ush200
billion was additional, was financed by the higher than expected domestic revenue
(Ush65 billion) and external grants (Ushl40 billion). Since the prior action allowed for
adjustments within the year o f the budget envelope with the approval o f the Parliament,
and the additional spending was financed entirely from additional tax revenues and
external grants, objective (i) under theprior action was assessed to have been met.

held each year, culminating in the National Public Expenditure Review in May each year based on the
issues andbudget proposals presented inthe National Budget Framework Paper.

51.        Expenditures in the security sector and the public administration sector
were both well above the approvedbudget. Actual expenditures in the security sector
exceeded the approved budget by 8.1 percent, with all votes in the sector (defense,
internal security and external security) exceeding their approved budget. The Public
Administration (PRSC definition) also spent above the approved budget by 14 percent,
largely owing to overspending by Parliament (39 percent), State House (26 percent),
Missions Abroad (14 percent), ForeignAffairs (9 percent), URA (6 percent) and Office o f
the President (6 percent). Based on the above, objective (iii) under theprior action was
deemed not to have been met.

52.        Controlling the growth of and over-spendingof the public administration
budget has remained problematic over almost a decade, with over-spending
averaging about 9 percent. These overruns were on top o f an average growth in
approved allocations to public administration o f 11.5 percent per year in nominal terms
over the last decade. In fact, the overruns in each year during the last decade effectively
ledto an increased allocation for the public administration sector inthe subsequent year.
This is the third time that under the PRSC series in which the prior action on limiting
supplementaryrequests to Parliament by the Security and Public Administration sectors
has not been met.

53.        In order to reverse this trend, the Governmentis slowly reducingthe share
of Public Administration in the total budget by 0.5 percentagepoints per year as
agreed during PRSC6. The share o f Public Administration in the FY2007/08 GOU
budget is 10 percent, down from 10.7 percent in the FY2006/07 budget. The share is
projected to further decline to 8.1 percent inFY2008/09. The pace o f reducing the share
o f the public administration budget exceeds what was called for under PRSC6, which
reflects the MoFPED commitment to holding down the share. It is also encouraging that
the data on budget releases also confirms this commitment to reduce public


                                                  28

 administrationexpenditure-with the share declining from 12.7 percent in FY2005/06
to 11.5percentinFY2006/07.
