     PEAPPILLAR2-ENHANCING                      PRODUCTION,COMPETITIVENESSAND INCOMES


PEAPStrategic Objective2.1-Efficientandcompetitiveprivatesector

54.        PRSC supports medium-term outcome 2.1.1, increased private sector
competitiveness.Improvingthe investment climate in Ugandaremains a key priority in
improving Uganda's competitiveness and increasing the level of private sector
investment. Uganda is ranked 118 in 2007 out of 178 economies in the cost of doing



                                                       29

business report 2008, slippingmarginally but consistently when compared with the 2007
and 2006 rankings (107 o f 175 economies in the 2007 report) as more countries have
focused on improving their business environment. Improving the entry and exit
requirements, property registrationand access to financial services will lead to sustainable
formal sector employment and a competitive private sector. Business entry and exit
regulations raise the rates o f new business creation. By way o f example Malaysia has 15
formally registered business per 100 citizens whereas Ugandahas less than 1 according to
the data obtained from the UgandaBusinesses RegistrationBureau.

55.      The Government's reforms are focused on improving entry and exit o fbusinesses
which would lead to a reduction o f entry and exit cost. The number o f steps for
registering formal businesses and exit should be reduced by revising the Companies Act
and the Insolvency Acts and reforming the UgandaBusiness Registration Bureau. Agreed
key actions under PRSC7 were:

     0  the Companies billandInsolvencybillto be approvedbythe Cabinet and
        presented to Parliament;

     0  the Policy Paper on Pension Reform to bepresentedto andapproved bythe
        Cabinet.
56.      Progress on prior action: The Government has made substantial progress in
improving the investment climate. Specifically, the Insolvency and Companies Bills were
prepared and submitted to the MOFPED for financial implications certification. Both are
expected to be approved by cabinet by April 2008 and submitted to parliament thereafter.
The draft Companies Bill proposes to reduce the number o f steps and cost involved in
setting up a business, and is expected to contribute to the formalization o f the economy.
To implement the new laws the Government is also strengthening the Uganda Business
Registration Bureau, by appointing a new Board and restructuring the Bureau.                 In
addition, the Government successfilly revised the labour laws. Uganda remains among
the top performers inthis category o fthe Cost o fDoingBusiness (ranked 11th).

57.      The Government i s also in the process o f revising the Mortgage Act, with draft
revision considered by the Parliament in 2007. The new Mortgage Bill was returned to
the Ministry o f Lands Housing and Urban Development for further revisions to take into
account recommendations o f stakeholders (Banking sector) without which the bill could
have had severe repercussions for the nascent mortgage market inUganda. It is currently
underdiscussion bythe Works committee o fthe Parliament.

58.      Inthe financial sector, two key activities to improve the financial sector are the
credit reference bureau (CRB) and pension reform. Over the PRSC6 period, the Bank of
Uganda licensed the credit reference bureau and procured a service provider. The launch
o f the CRB, originally scheduled for September 2007, was delayed mainly because the
provider had to sort out regulatory issues within his home country. On sorting these out,
the provider has since formulated an identification system by way o f a financial card
carrying biometric data, and i s inthe final stages o f signing service level agreements with
participating financial institutions. It i s expected that the CRB will be filly operational by
May 2008. Inthe pension area, the reform process that hadbeen stalled for a long time i s


                                                30

 being rekindled. Following a review o f the pension reform process, government decided
 to strategically adopt a stepwise approach by puttinginplace a regulatory framework for
 the sector, before liberalization. Hence, a. policy paper on pension sector regulation has
 beendiscussed and approved by Cabinet at the end ofFebruary 2008. This is an important
 first step as it will lead to new legislation to be enacted to establish an independent
 regulatory authority for all pension fimds - public or private. However for the reform
 process to be complete two important steps are necessary (i)for public pensions -
 converting the public service pension scheme into a sustainable fund, and (ii) private      for
 pensions - liberalizing the sector by allowing reputable occupational funds to cease
 contributing to the NSSF. Cabinet approval for the overall pension reform strategy i s not
 expected untilJune 2008.

 59.      Government recognizes the importanceof the investmentclimate agenda in
 the PRSC framework. Promotion o f investment is not only vital for poverty reduction
 by creating jobs, but also for revenue generation to finance government programs. The
 CICS matrix is continuously reviewed to ensure that it continues to highlight the key
 reform areas. The annual prioritization o f the keyreform areas inthis matrix will continue
 to form the basis for future PRSC triggers.

 PEAPStrategicObjective2.2 -Increasedand moreefficient agricultural
 production

 60.      The Plan for the Modernization o f Agriculture (PMA) is a multi-sectoral policy
 and institutional framework put in place by the Government in 2001 to address
 constraints to production, incomes and competitiveness in rural areas. It i s the key
mechanism in the current PEAP to implement and coordinate actions across several
 sectors, and the associated ministries and agencies, to achieve this strategic objective. The
goal o f P M A i s to transform agriculture from a largely subsistence orientation to more
market-oriented production and commercial farming through coordinated interventions in
seven priority areas." Since PRSC3, prior actions for rural development have been based
on the undertakings agreed at the P M A Joint Annual Review (JAR).

61.       The total number o f undertakings across seven pillars o f PMA, however, is very
large. Given the challenge o f addressing multiple constraints in a transforming
agriculture, the need for actions on many fronts i s understandable, but it also makes it
difficult to monitor and assess effective progress against the PMA and PEAP outcomes.18
To help focus on higher priority areas (among the undertakings), for PRSC6 the Bank
team, key Government agencies and other Development Partners agreed to focus on a
smaller set o f six high priority undertakings as a more substantive basis for assessing
progress against the rural development prior action. Usingthe same rationale, for PRSC7


17. The seven priority areas or "Pillars" of the PMA are: agricultural research; agricultural advisory
services; agricultural education; rural financial services; marketing and ago-processing; natural resource
management; and rural infrastructure.
18. For example, for PRSC6, which reported progress on undertakings discussed during the PMA JAR o f
2005, it was noted that a total o f 58 undertakings were agreedupon (inthe Ph4A JAR of 2004), of which 20
(34 percent) actions were completed while the rest (66 percent) were either at various stages o f
implementation or hadnot beencompleted.


                                                     31

 it was agreed to further sharpen the focus on four priority actions o f greatest strategic
 importanceto achieve significant agricultural growth andpoverty reduction.

 62.      Varying degrees o f progress was achieved on each o f the seven pillars o f PMA
 prior to PRSC 7. The most significant achievements were made inthe implementation o f
 the National Agricultural Advisory Services (NAADS), and the establishment o f a new
 and significantly reformed National Agricultural Research System (NARS). Substantial
progress was also made in the environment sector, with the development and
 implementation o f environmental impact assessment guidelines for several key sectors,
 incorporation o f district environmental action plansinthe District Development Plans for
 several districts, and the implementation o f the National Forestry Plan (including the
 establishment o f the National Forestry Authority). Other notable achievements (for PRSC
 5 and 6 periods) include: the development o f an agricultural marketing and agro-
processing strategy; the operations o f a market information system (MIS) beingpiloted in
 six districts with important lessons for further establishment o f a sustainable MIS (taken
up as a highpriority area for PRSC 7); standards having been established for maize and
beans in line with the East African regional standards to facilitate trade in these
commodities; the passage o f the Warehouse Receipts Act by Parliament; the
incorporation of the National Agricultural Education Strategy in the Education Sector
Strategic Plan; submission o f the LandUse Policy to the Cabinet; strengthening the basis
for a stronger foundation for tenure security for women (through the completion o f a
baseline assessment survey o f awareness levels o f women's land rights) and the
finalization o f the Environment and Natural Resources Strategy and Investment Plan.
Progress has also beenmade inother areas, but the pace has been slower than anticipated,
either because o f unrealistic prior expectations or the politically sensitive nature o f the
proposed reforms. These include the computerization o f the Land Registry records for
Kampala, Wakiso and Mpigi and the piloting o f systematic demarcation o f land in three
districts.

63.       Two areas discussed under PRSC 6 where continued attention i s needed, with
important results expected in coming months, are: undertaking the Agricultural Census,
pending for many years, as a critical step for providing the much needed evidence base
for current and future policy and strategy decisions; and completion o f an Agriculture
Sector PER. Both o f these are critical inputs into the revision o f the MAAIF
Development Strategy and Investment Plan, and the planned restructuring o f MAAIF.
The PER will also help assess the effectiveness o f current public expenditures on
agriculture, as well as their adequacy, and help to align public expenditures to PEAP
priorities and outcomes.

64.      Given this experience, and the decision to focus on fewer but more strategic
actions, it has been agreed for PRSC 7 to focus attention on areas and pillars o f PMA that
have lagged in implementation, acknowledging the substantial progress that has been
achieved in implementing the agricultural research and advisory services Pillars (i.e. the
productivity agenda). The focus in PRSC7 i s thus on the three sub-areas o f improved
resource utilization, irrigation, and agricultural marketing that have the potential for large
impacts on rural growth and poverty reduction. In addition, for the sector as a whole,
following up on the PRSC6 prior action on initiating the Agriculture Sector PER and


                                              32

improving resource utilization was also identified as an area o f focus, which shouldbe set
within the context o f the sector Development Strategy and Investment Plan (DSIP) due
for revision this year.

65.      The continued lack of harmonizationamong rural development strategies
(PMA, PFA, and RDS) remainsan area of serious concern.The specific prior actions
listed below are premised on a clearly articulated policy and institutional framework for
agriculture and rural development. As noted above, the PMA has been such a policy
framework for the PEAP. Over the past about two years, however, new initiatives for
rural development have beenintroduced, namely the Rural Development Strategy (RDS)
andthe Prosperityfor All (PFA) program. These have introduced a lack of clarity on the
guidinginstitutional and policy framework for rural development, an issue highlightedin
the PRSC6 program document and reiterated during the PRSC7 missions. All these
programs have almost identical objectives with overlapping mandates, but with unclear
and potentially parallel implementation arrangements and significant scope for
duplication o f efforts and wastage o f development resources. There is a critical need to
achieve consensus on a consistent and coordinated policy framework for agriculture and
rural development to avoid adverse impacts on the implementation o f current programs
and the ad-hoc introduction o f new and potentially inconsistent activities. Moving
forward, it i s essential. to incorporate the lessons from the experience o f PEAP andPMA
to ensure that the basic principles and tested best practices that have underpinned
Uganda's good performance over the past two decades are not compromised, while the
gaps in the strategy and its implementation are filled to accelerate rural growth and
poverty reduction. There remains an urgent need for the Government to clarify its policy
and institutional framework for guiding rural development actions and to ensure that
PRSC7 objectives on rural development will be met.

66.      Urgency to undertake the agricultural census. Lack o f accurate data and
information was also identified in PRSC6 as a critical constraint to sectoral and policy
decisions for the agricultural sector. The last comprehensive agricultural census was
undertaken over 40 years ago. In this light, MoFPED allocated resources to start an
agricultural census during FY2007/08. It i s now a high priority for UBOS to begin as
soon as possible. The current plans for the census, with activities spanning the next five
years, need to be revisedto shorten the timeframe andensure that the census i s carried out
as early as possible. UBOS has sought Technical Assistance to review the design and
strategy for the Agriculture Census, which i s expected to start inSeptember, 2008.

67.      The specific prior actionsthat will bethe focus for PRSC7 include:

0   Improved resource utilization and allocation for the agricultural sector.
    Adequate budgetresources and their efficient use are a frequent source o f debate with
    MoFPED. Also, the current MAAIF DSIP that covers the period 2005/06 to 2007/08
    i s due to be revised. This i s essential to ensure that MAAIF's activities remain
    relevant to the agreed priorities for the sector inthe coming medium-term period. Key
    inputsto improve the use and allocation o fresources andthe DSIP are the Agriculture
    Sector Public Expenditure Review (PER) and the CAADP stocktaking exercise. The
    Bank and DFID are providing support for the agricultural PER. To ensure that these


                                               33

  processes stay on track, it was agreed with MAAIF that its SBFP for FY2008/09 due
  inJanuary 2008 (revised from the initial date of December 2007 due to the delay in
  the National Budget process this year) and the finalization o f the framework for
  revision o f MAAIF DSIP, scheduled for the end o f December 2008, will both
  incorporate the main findings o f the ongoing agricultural PER. This undertaking i s on
  track. The revised DSIP, informed by findings o f the PER and CAADP processes, i s
  envisaged to be a firm and objective basis for resource allocation inthe sector leaving
  little or no room for new initiatives inan ad hoc manner.


0 Water for agricultural production. Uganda is better endowed with rainfall than
  most o f its neighbors, yet virtually all o f the county's agricultural output depends on
  rainfed agriculture. This makes the livelihood o f the rural poor highly vulnerable to
  periodic weather shocks, with frequent low agricultural production and high levels o f
  food insecurity in several parts o f the country. With the transfer o f the mandate for
  Water for Agricultural Production to MAAIF, it was initially agreed that MAAIF
  would finalize and submit a draft Water for Production Policy to the PMA Steering
  Committee (a preliminary step before going to the Cabinet) by December 2007.
  Subsequently, MAAIF will submit the policy to the Cabinet and finalize a National
  Water for Agricultural Production SIP by June 2008. At the discussion o f the
  inception report for this work, however, the SWG was guided by the Minister for
  Agriculture, to significantly broaden the scope o f the background field work to ensure
  a regionally differentiated and more relevant National Policy.           As a result the
  completion date for this prior action has been delayed but process remains on track,
  with the draft Policy to be ready by September 2008 and the SIP to be ready by end-
  2008. The new delivery dates are, however, well beyond the critical milestones for
  PRSC7 and this undertaking i s thus recorded as delayed.


0 Establishmentof an agriculturalmarket informationsystem. Provision o f timely
  and relevant marketing information to farmers and other key players in the sector
  remains a major constraint in the agricultural sector. This issue has been a matter o f
  concern and study for a number o f years, but with little concrete progress. The pilots
  tested in 6 districts in 2005 proved to be too costly to be sustained through public
  finding. A study commissioned in 2006 on the way forward recommended
  establishing a Public-Private Partnership for the operation o f a sustainable MIS. The
  concerned agencies, MTTI, MAAIF,and MoFPED, have thus agreed to establish such
  a sustainable MIS over the next two years. The key steps are for MTTI, in
  collaboration with MAAIFand other agencies (including potential users o f the MIS),
 to develop a public-private partnership framework with clearly defined roles for the
 public and private sectors, and to establish a sustainable Agricultural Market
 Information System framework. The draft Public-Private Partnership framework has
 been completed and is to be discussed at a stakeholders workshop in Kampala on
 February 5, 2008. Inaddition, the existing MIS (currently runby CIAT and fimdedby
 DANIDA with short term finding) has beensuccessfully tendered for operation by a
 private company. The contract for this partnership i s awaiting the finalization o f the
 PPP framework after the Technical Consultations.             The implementation o f the




                                            34

    program will begin in early 2008, with a fully functioning system in place by
    December 2008. This undertakingis thus assessed to have been on track.

68.      Land reforms:. Reforms in the land sub-sector are crucial to provide farmers
tenure security, a critical factor for improving agricultural productivity through increased
investments and higher production efficiency. An outstanding issue for the sector i s
finalization o f the land policy, which has been pending for several years. While a draft
landpolicy has beendeveloped, it is a very sensitive and controversial subject, requiring
extensive consultations with stakeholders in different regions, as well as different interest
groups, before it can be finalized. To ensure that this process is brought to a conclusion,
MLHUD agreed to complete the remaining regional and special interest group
consultations complete the draft land policy, hold a National Land Policy Conference ,
and submit the draft land policy to the Cabinet by July 1, 2008, with an action plan in
place to start implementation in FY2009/10.            MLHUD has undertaken several
consultations, but has had to suspend further consultations in light o f the tensions and
public controversy surrounding the Amendments to the current Land Bill recently
introduced in the Parliament.        Some setback in the consultative process was also
experienced with the outbreak o f Ebola in Western Uganda, and thus, submission o f the
landpolicy to the Cabinet will be delayedby a few months


                         PEAPPILLAR4 -GOODGOVERNANCE
PEAPStrategicObjective4.4 -Strengthenedpublicsector managementand
accountability

69.      Improving the public financial management systems has been a major
priority for the Government of Uganda (GOU). It is a core component o f the PRSC
prior actions over the years. PRSCs have helped to support an improved regulatory
framework for public finance," organizational structure o f the office o f the Accountant
General, establish a coordination mechanism to guide and monitor public financial
management reforms, and ensure that yearly budget execution reports provide details o f
actual expenditures and not budget releases.

70.      Modernization o f the regulatory framework has been accompanied by
modernization and updating o f systems and capacity building for staff o f the Ministry o f
Finance. A government-wide Integrated Financial Management System (IFMS) was
successfully rolled out to all 18 central government ministries and nine local
governments. Plans are underway to roll out the IFMS to the remaining agencies and
higher local governments. This roll out has improved both the quality and timeliness o f
accounts. The 2005/06 audited accounts show that central government accounting
procedures are gradually being brought in line with the framework o f the International
Public Sector Accounting Standards (IPSAS). GOU adopted the Electronic Funds
Transfer system from July 2,2007 for all payments to central and local government (to be


19. The Public Finance and Accountability Act (PFAA), 2003; Financial Regulations and Treasury
Accounting Instructions.



                                             35

done in a phased manner starting with the districts/municipalities already using IFMS).
This is an excellent achievement for which the Government needs to be commended.
However there are associated fiduciary risks that need to be addressed, as brought out in
the IFMSPost ImplementationReport prepared by the Auditor General.

71.      Improving the PFM system still has risks and challenges. The Public
Expenditure and Financial Accountability (PEFA) Report o f November 2005 (issued in
July 2006) assessed the performance o f the PFM system and, inter alia, identified some o f
the main areas o f concernzo:(i) and timeliness o f in-year budget reports because
                                        quality
budget reports only include information on budget releases and not actual expenditures;
(ii)stock andmonitoringofexpenditurepaymentarrearsremainachallengewithnew
arrears arising due to poor budgeting practices and significant under provisioning in the
areas o f pensions, court awards, utilities and subscriptions to international organizations;
(iii)effectiveness o f internal audits; (iv) oversight o f aggregate fiscal risks from other
public sector entities; (v) effectiveness o f measures for taxpayer registration and tax
assessments; (vi) legislative scrutiny o f external audit reports; (vii) effectiveness o f
payroll controls; and (viii) effectiveness o ftax collection. The Government o fUganda has
prepared a Financial Management Accountability Program (FINMAP) to address the
weaknesses in the PFM system. The proposed budget for 2007/08 is approximately US$
15 million. It i s important to note that PFM improvements take time and the next PEFA
assessmentwill be done inNovember 2008 when these ratings will be reassessed.

72.      The report and opinions of the Auditor General on the public accounts of
the Republic of Uganda for the year ending June 30, 2006 have been submitted to
Parliament. The current accounting system is much improved, but the number o f
unqualified (clean) opinions in central government needs to be improved. Out o f the 85
central government institutions audited, only 18 (21 percent) had unqualified (clean)
opinions, 66 (78 percent) had qualified (except for) opinions, and one account (1 percent)
had a disclaimer opinion. This i s a matter o f concern and there i s clearly a need for
improved financial discipline to ensure that more fiiture audits receive more clean
opinions. The Audit Report also states that around 50 percent o f central government
ministries (including the Ministry o f Finance, Planning and Economic Affairs) did not
submit their annual financial statements within the statutorily prescribed period three
months after the close o fthe financial year (September 30,2006).

74.     The PRSC7 Prior Action is geared toward makingavailable informationand data
on public spending, improving payroll controls andpayroll audits, financial reporting and
audits o f parastatals, and clearing o f backlog of Treasury Memoranda. Steady progress
has been made on these undertakingsas detailed above:

PublicProcurement

75.     Strengtheningpublicprocurementis centralto enhancingbudgetexecution,
achieving value for money, and the fight against erosive corruption. The PRSC6




                                                   39

procurement prior action covered review o f the procurement law," procurement planning,
and the follow-up o f procurement audits, which are the measures needed to improve
effectiveness o f the public procurement system. Ongoing efforts aim to strengthen the
procurement law, build procurement capacity and develop a more effective method o f
conducting regular procurement audits. There i s a lingering concern about the lack o f
action on public procurement outside established procedures that could frustrate the
efforts o f institutions chargedwith oversight o fthe procurement system.

76.       The main goal of the public procurementlaw is to ensure that procurement
is carried out in a transparent and systematic manner that achieves value for
money, efficiency, equal opportunities to the bidding community, and fosters
developmentof the privatesector. The key areas of focus are revising the procurement
law, building procurement capacity in Procuring and Disposal Entities (PDEs),
monitoring compliance, and taking action in cases o f non-compliance. The following
areas have been identifiedas requiring attention:

             PPDA and stakeholders have identified some deficiencies in the public
             procurement law and its regulations that need to be revised in order to close
             gaps that lead to discretionary actions and corruption.

             A functioning Procurement andDisposal Unit(PDU) is keyto complyingwith
             the procurement law and processing inan efficient manner.A well functioning
             PDU should have a trainedtrainable procurement officer as its head, other
             staff as needed and adequateoffice and equipment.

             Procurement record keeping is poor. Poor record keeping undermines
             transparency and i s used to cover fraudulent procurement transactions.
             Accounting officers (heads o f PDEs) should be required to ensure adequate
             procurement records are kept as requiredby the procurement law.

             Procurement planning is not adequate and not enforced. In the absence o f
             adequate procurement planning, spending units could experience cash
             crunches or end up with idle balances, hindering effective budget execution
             andresultinginaccumulationo farrears or unplannedpurchases.

             Enforcement o f the procurement law i s weak. There are almost no actions
             taken on non-compliant cases identified by PPDA's procurement audits and
             by Auditor General's reports. Action on non-compliant cases is a major way
             o f raising the risk level to government officials who carry out procurement
             outside established procedures and through corrupt practices andor
             incompetence. Without enforcement, the law would not meet its objectives.
             The role o f integrity institutions (PPDA, IGG, and Auditor General) needs to
             be coordinated because they should complement each other when dealing with
             procurement corruption.
77.      There are a few other items that also need to be addressed: (i)PPDA should
complete and issue Standard Bidding Documents for use by PDEs; (ii)PPDA should


21. The Public Procurement and Disposal of Public Assets Act (2003) and The Public Procurement and
Disposal of Public Assets Regulations (2003).


                                               40

develop a more effective way o f handling capacity buildingand conducting procurement
audits; and (iii)    to strengthen enforcement, PPDA should prepare more comprehensive
reports on all identified offences under section 95 o f the PPDA Act, with realistic
recommendations on actions to be taken by PS/ST, the head o f Public Service, Auditor
General, IGG, CID, andDPP.

78.      The Government needs an effective system for measuring performance inpublic
procurement in addition to the OECDDAC pilot program,'* focusing on monitoring the
following areas:

     (0     Efficiencyof the publicprocurement system. Data should include how long
            the various steps o f the procurement process take, how much o f the allocated
            budget under a procurement plan i s executed, and the adequacy o f the
            hnctionality o f PDUs, the procurement record keeping system, and
            procurement planning.

    (ii) Valueformoney.Asampleofpurchaseditemshontractsshouldbereviewed
            to see whether contract prices are reasonable and consistent with market rates
            across the procuring entities.
    (iii) Compliance with procurement law. Data should indicate percentage of
            contracts audited not complying with the procurement law. Enforcement can be
            measured on basis o f actions taken on non-compliant cases.

    (W      Transparency. The amount and content o f information made available to the
            public through publications or on the PPDA website will indicate the degree o f
            transparency.

79.      The prior action i s geared toward (i)  revampingthe legal framework that governs
                                                        ~~

public procurement in order to close gaps that lead to -discretionary actions and
corruption; and (ii)    ensuringthe establishment o f hnctioning procurement and disposals
unitsinGovernment bodies.

80.      The prior actions have not been fully met. Revisions to the Public
Procurement Law and its Regulations have been delayed beyond PRSC7 approval. The
PPDA prepared a draft o f the proposed revisions but it is incomplete and does not cover
all the provisions that needed to be revised. The Government has determined that
additional time and professional input are necessary to come up with sound revisions that
would bring the law and regulations to acceptable standards. It has formed an inter-
agency task force and is working under a revised schedule to complete the draft
amendments for submission to Cabinet by September 30, 2008. Given the importance o f
this reform, the Government proposes to include this undertaking as one o f the triggers
for the release o f the second tranche funding under PRSC7. PDUs and Contracts
Committees in central government ministries have been established and are operational.
In Local Governments, there is good progress in the establishment of contracts
committees with 99 percent (92 out o f 93 LGs) having established Contracts Committees.
However only 50 percent have recruited procurement officers at all levels, which


22. Uganda is a pilot country for OECDiDACprocurement benchmarking.


                                               41

presumes that the Local Government would be in a position to establish a PDU, while
remaining LocalGovernments are at different stages o fthe recruitment process.

81.        In view of the mixedprogressin procurement,the Government agreedto an
additional undertakingto compensate for the weak progress on the revisionof the
law. The PPDA has reviewed about 200 contracts selected from a cross section o f
Procuring Entities, covering works, goods and services, to assess the extent to which the
contracts were procured following the procedures in the procurement law. The Interim
Report on the procurement contracts reviewed to date by PPDA, submitted to IDA during
negotiations shows high percentage o f contracts which are not fully compliant with the
established procurement procedure^.^^ In view o f this finding, the PPDA intends to
continue reviewing contracts at random to strengthen compliance with the law, with the
target o freviewing 1000contracts by late 2008.

PEAPStrategicObjective4.4.2 -Reducedcorruption

82.        The anti-corruption program in Uganda is anchored in the National Anti-
corruption Strategy (NACS) and requires crosscutting reforms o f public service, public
finance, procurement, and local government to eliminate factors that contribute to
corruption. The Government i s developing new legislationand strengthening existing one
to fight corruption and support implementation of the NACS. In addition during the
PEAP period in question the Government has focused on strengthening o f institutions
that fight corruption.

Background

83.        The PRSC 5 and 6 prior actions focused on the effective implementation o f the
NACS. During PRSC 5 emphasis was put on the revision of the Leadership Code Act
(LCA 2002), following Parliament's passing o f the Constitutional Amendment Bill o f
2005, which retained the powers o f the Inspector General o f Government (IGG) and
approved the setting up o f the Special Anti Corruption Court and a Leadership Code
Tribunal.

84.        Under PRSC6 the Government committed to planning for the third National
Integrity Survey (NIS) to measure the incidence o f corruption and analyze the trends in
the fight against corruption since the 2002 survey.                            In addition a joint
Government/development partner task force was set up to steer the process o f
establishing the special anti-corruption courts and develop mechanisms to reduce the
corruption and governance-related case backlog in the IGG's office and the judiciary.
The implementationo fthis agendai s monitored under PRSC 7.




23 The February 2008 review o f public procurement contracts undertaken by PPDA revealed that out o f
200 contracts, 74 percent had not fully met at least one of the seven criteria for f i l l compliance with
established procedures. In terms o f contract value, over 80 percent was found non-compliant with at least
one criterion.


                                                     42

 PRSC7prior actions

 85.      Support for the fight against corruption continues to be a priority for PRSC7,
 with additional focus on improving synergies among accountability agencies and
 empowering the civil society in its monitoring and advocacy roles. Priorities include
 continuation o f the development and review o f the legislation, development o f a new
NACS, operationalization o f the Anti Corruption Division o f the High Court and the
 Leadership Code Tribunal, and enhanced fundingo fthe anti-corruption agencies.

 86.      The October 2006 Joint Accountability Sector Review highlighted a number o f
 interventions and benchmarks to be achieved by October 2007, and although a follow-up
 formal sector review has yet to take place, discussions with Government indicate that
 commendable progress has been achieved on the development o f new legislation and
review o f existing laws. Review of the Prevention o f Corruption Act (revised) has been
completed and a new and expanded bill, "the Anti Corruption Bill" (2008) has been
tabled in February2008. The Whistleblower Protection Bill i s expected to be submitted
to Cabinet shortly.     It is important to note that both Bills have received financial
implications certificates from MoFPED. The Directorate o f Public Prosecution (DPP) i s
developing a Bill to support Witness Protection and complement the Whistleblower
Protection Bill. There has been no progress on amending the Leadership Code Act
because it i s the subject o f a pending appeal inthe Supreme Court (following a ruling o f
the Constitutional Court). Government i s delaying the work on the Qui Tam Bill to
follow the Whistleblower Protection Bill for more effective implementation. It i s
anticipated that the Qui Tam Bill will be presented to Cabinet in July, 2008. Further,
draft regulations to operationalise the Access to Information Act (2005) have been
completed. However, the anti-corruption agencies continue to have capacity constraints
and lack resources, which affect their ability to deliver on their mandates effectively. In
addition to pending court cases, lengthy approval and implementation processes for
legislationhave ledto slow progress inimplementingthe anti-corruption agenda.

87.      The recent budget provided a slight increase in allocations to anti-corruption
agencies and development partners are preparing parallel support programs. The
accountability sector has been formally established, a coordinator recruited and a sector
investment plan i s under preparation. This is an important step to ensure a coordinated
approach inthe fight against corruption.

88.      The consultative process to develop the next NACS has been launched. The
next NACS i s expected to focus on (i)   improving collaboration among the accountability
agencies by revampingand strengtheningthe Inter-Agency Forum (IAF) and establishing
a clear link to the accountability sector working group; (ii) seeking closer collaboration
with civil society that will be expected to play greater monitoring and advocacy roles in
the implementation o f the NACS; (iii)   effectively implementing legislation that supports
the strategy by operationalizing the various acts; and (iv) strengthening the capacity o f
accountability agencies through improved budget allocations to enable the necessary
capacity to fulfill their mandates. It i s envisioned that preparation o f NACS will be
concluded inApril 2008.




                                             43

 89.     The Inspectorate of the Government (IG) has embarked on the third
NationalIntergritySurvey(NIS). It is expected that future PRSCs will be able to utilize
findings from the survey to focus more on the problematic areas revealed by the survey.
Further, results from the customized survey will yield important information to help the
Inspectorate o f Government to reshape its strategy. Consistency with past surveys is
important to facilitate trend analyses. The N I S should be completed by June 2008. The
findings o f the second N I S highlightedseveral challenges that have to some extent been
addressedby the Government. These include but are not limitedto:

   (9    The need to strengthen the IG and carry out more public awareness o f its role to
         which the government responded by increased funding. As a result the IG has
         been able to carry out more public awareness programs on the dangers o f
         corruption.

  (ii) The need for the IGto focus more on preventivemechanisms to combat
         corruption. The IG has responded by putting emphasis on policy and systems
         studies incorruption-prone institutions (so far studies have been conducted inthe
         National Social Security Fund and Land Registry) and will also be a point o f
         focus for the third NIS.
 (iii) The need to amend the local government act section 64, and have CAOs
         recruited by the Public Service Commission to ensure they are not subjected to
         political influence, which has been a problem. In addition, the findings o f the
         second NIS reinforced the need to reform the URA and the police following two
         commissions o finquiryinto the operations o f these two agencies.
90.      Good progress has been made toward establishing the special Anti-Corruption
Division o f the high court. The legal status o f the proposed Anti-Corruption Court was
discussed at the highest level both inthe Judiciary and Parliament.The joint task force
chaired by the Principal Judge concludes that the court should be set up administratively
as a division o f the High Court (Anti-Corruption Division, ACD) as was done with the
Commercial Court. The ACD will start as a four-year pilot and have four regional courts
at lower level to respondto cases. A multi stakeholder taskforce has been constituted to
ensure that the following critical steps are finalized: (i)
                                                          development and gazetting o f the
instrument establishing the ACD; (ii)  review and issuing o f new rules for the operation o f
the ACD; (iii) adequate funding and staffing, including appointment o fjudges by
                secure
the President for the pilot phase; and (iv) find suitable premises, equipment and furniture
for the court. Despite these numerous matters to be worked out the GOU has set an
ambitious target of having the court operational by July 1, 2008.

91.     Whereas the Government committed to reviewing the corruption cases backlog
alongside the establishment o f the ACD, progress on this exercise has been slow. The
consultants hired to support this process have been requested to work out a plan o f action
for clearing the backlog and preventing further accumulation.        A one-stop centre to
receive and monitor cases has been proposed. The IGG's office believes that this issue
needs to be tackled intandemwith operationalization o fthe anti-corruption court.




                                              44

PEAP Strategic Objective4.4.3 -Improvedpublicsector performance

Background

92.      Under PRSC 5 the GOU committed to developing an updated pay reform
strategy and target salary adjustments for the public service. The Ministry o f Public
Service (MOPS) finalized a new policy that was approved by Cabinet in August 2006.
The policy provided for enhanced medium term pay targets for all grades o f staff and
proposed an incentive structure for public servants in hard to reach areas. This was part
o fthe overall implementation o f the revampedPublic Service Reform Program (PSRP).

93.      Under PRSC 6, the GOU committed to a wage bill study to identify policy and
strategic measures needed to control the size o f the public service, achieve efficiency in
wage bill utilization, enable implementation o f the pay reform policy and eliminate the
public service pension arrears in the medium to long term. This has informed further
work on payreform monitoredunderPRSC 7.

Prior actionunderPRSC7

94.      As part o f monitoring the implementation o fthe the new phase o f the PSRP, the
undertakings for sector/program review based on the PSRP Key Results Matrix (KRM)
were agreed on inNovember 2006. The first annual joint review o f the undertakingstook
place inMarch 2008. The agreed undertakings include interventions that will seek to : (i)
enhance the performance and accountability o f public service organizations; (ii)       attract
andretainadequatenumber o f skilled and able personnel inthe public service; (iii) in   put
place a public service that i s efficient and affordable; (iv) provide the public service with
an enabling and empowering work environment; (v) motivate and adequately remunerate
public servants; and (vi) develop leadership andchange management skills throughout the
public service. Although achievements are visible under each o f these areas, the overall
implementation progress remains inordinately slow and not all KRM actions have been
met. The DPshave expressed concern about the recent development, i.e., that the Bank's
project supporting the public service reforms has been cancelled due to a failure by
Government to meet the condition o f effectiveness during an eighteen month period.
They believe that this sends an unclear signal on GOU's total commitment to improving
the performance o fits public service.

95.      In addition to monitoring the overall performance of the PSRP through the
above mentioned KRM, the GOU as part o f the PRSC 7 prior action committed to: (i)
follow-up actions and especially sanctions for those responsible for ghosts employees
identified during last year's payroll cleaning exercise; (ii)addressing the capacity
constraint reflected in vacant professionaVexecutive posts in the MOPS structure; (iii)
specific measures to achieve strategic objectives and efficiency inthe management o f the
wage bill and human resources; and (iv) progress toward attaining the medium-term
salary targets contained inthe 2006 public service pay reform policy.

96.      A sub-component o f the PSRP that has received special attention in successive
PRSCs has been improving Government payroll management. The 2006 payroll cleaning



                                              45

 exercise revealed major leaks in the payroll system, including the existence o f "ghost"
 workers. Besides reinforcing controls in the payroll system and removing the ghost
 workers, it was anticipated that the Government would take effective punitive measures
 to sanction the officers responsible for perpetrating the presence o f ghosts and other
 fraudulent and wasteful payments through the payroll. In March, 2007, the Permanent
 Secretary/Secretary to the Treasury directed all accounting officers to account for the
 salaries that were paid to the invalid records found on the payroll. Due to the poor
 response, in October, 2007, the Permanent Secretary o f the MoPS requested accounting
 officers to submit the accountability by November 15,2007. Submission and analysis o f
 the accountabilities have been completed and the MoPS has advised the Head o f Public
 Service and Secretary to Cabinet, the PS M o L G and the Secretary to the PSC to institute
 disciplinary action against nine accounting officers in MDAs and 49 accounting officers
 from LGs ,who did not account for the funds.

 97.      A number o f cases have been handled by the police resulting in imprisonment
and/or interdiction in the M o W T (3), Masaka District (4) and Kasese District (1). These
actions are a step in the right direction by Government and will serve as a deterrence
against similar offenses in the future. It i s therefore critical that Government does not
fa1ter.

98.       Related to the payroll cleaning exercise, MoFPED/Accountant General has
undertaken a payroll audit o f Ministries and Government departments.             Whereas the
nature and scope o f this i s different from the earlier payroll cleaning exercise, there seems
to be substantial overlap in the issues identified. This points to the benefits o f closer
collaboration in this area between MoPS and MoFPED/Accountant General. The same
point could be made regarding the verification o f the pension arrears. The audits have
highlighted several cases o fpayroll management irregularities including (i)    weaknesses in
payroll business processes and controls and (ii)    irregularities in the payroll. These cases
require immediate action, especially the cases o f salary overpayment and delays to effect
deletion from the payroll which may result in ghost workers. The MoPS will pursue this
together with the Accountant General's office and agree on actions to address these
anomalies. Further, to enhance the integrity o f the payroll, MoPS i s gearing for the
implementation o f the Integrated Personnel and Payroll System (PPS). The process o f
procuring a long term advisor to support this process has been slow and the system
software solution will be delayed until about August 2008.

99.      As reflected in the policy dialogue and benchmark actions in successive past
PRSCs, GOU has beencognizant o f the need to enhance public service pay to levels that
will enable recruitment, retention, and motivation o f highly qualified personnel.
However, reaching target pay levels has not been possible since the policy objective was
established in the early 199Os,yet the aggregate wage bill has generally been rising each
year at a rate that exceeds that o f economic growth.

100.     The major challenges in the policy targets o f the Government's public service
pay reform strategy include (i)     the size and growth o f the public service, which are at
levels that outpace the budget/MTEF allocation to the total wage bill; (ii)          pursuit o f
MDGs indicates the case for more rapid growth o fthe public service to select sub-sectors


                                               46

for service delivery; and (iii)       utilization o f the available limited wage bill and human
resources i s generally not in line with the strategic priorities o f an efficiently and
effectively managed public service. In the context o f these challenges, GOU
commissioned a study o f the wage bill and planning and budgeting for human resources
inpursuit of the medium-term salary targets contained inthe public service pay reform
policy adopted by the Cabinet last year. This study has now been completed, and MoPS
and MoFPED are currently reviewing its findings and recommendations. MoFPED and
MoPS have a team o f senior officers to develop a technically and politically feasible
scenario for addressing the challenges outlined above.

101.      The issue remains complicated. A large share o f the budget already pays salaries
o f public sector workersYz4particularly in the social sectors where service delivery i s
direct contact with the beneficiaries. Lowering employee costs o f service delivery i s not a
simple matter o f reducing inputs or increasing outputs, but requires in tandem quality
improvements for better final outcomes. Public sector employees need to be empowered
and motivated to create public goods that ultimately contribute to growth and shared
prosperity. A way must be found to reward them with better remuneration in return for
delivering clearly defined service delivery outcomes.

102.      Wages alone constituted almost one-third o f the total budget in FY2006/07, but
moving to a higher pay scale (pay reform) i s practically impossible unless large pockets
o f inefficiencies and leaks can be eliminated. Moving to a long-term pay target would
mean that human resource costs in the public sector would grow to about two-thirds o f
the budget, leaving little for operating costs and investments in critical physical
infrastructure. Furthermore, there are embedded exceptions inthe personnel system, some
o f which are considered by the beneficiaries as entitlements (pay above grade,
allowances, vehicle/fbel subsidy, autonomous agencies), that will limit maneuverability
around options that may be presented for civil service reform. The Bank i s currently
studyingpersonnel deployment across the Government in more detail to understand the
required tradeoffs in moving to an enhanced pay scale, as well as to consider an
appropriate sequence o f reforms that would minimize the cost o f a major restructuring
exercise. MOFPED and MOPS have joined efforts to identify specific and time-bound
measures to achieve (i)a right-sized public service, i.e., affordable and consistent with
strategic and efficient use o f the wage bill and human resources and delivery o f priority
social services, and (ii)   the medium-term salary targetsunder the GOUpay reform policy
approved by the Cabinet in 2006. The strategy for achieving these public service pay
refondenhancement targets is not yet ready. The inter-ministerial task team from the
MOFPED and MOPS that spearheads this exercise has yet to complete the rationalization
o f expenditures which i s the basis for determining the scope for enhancing the wage bill,
and thereby the approach and pace for pursuing the pay enhancement targets in the
medium-tern.



24 Wages constituted 31 percent of total budget, while other benefits such as allowances, health costs,
welfare and entertainment, and training added another 20 percentage points. Thus, over halfof the budget in
FY2006/07was devoted to public sector remuneration. Including the military and staffs of publicly funded
autonomous bodies, this figure would be even higher.


                                                   47

 103.     The task team has decided to have a more rigorous budgetrationalization process
than it had initially envisaged. This rationalization will entail the involvement of every
ministry in: (i)  providing information and data to inform the rationalization; and (ii)
demonstrating any potential negative consequences o f the proposed rationalization to its
operations. To the latter end, ministries have been required to provide more details on
their establishments and personnel expenditure budgets in the Budget Call Circular for
FY 2008-09.      In addition, the PS of MOPS also issued guidelines for budgeting for
salaries for the next fiscal year requiring Line Ministries and Local Governments to
indicate their wage estimates by program including a provision for newly recruited staff
not yet on payroll. This i s to facilitate budgeting andminimize requests for supplementary
funding. Meanwhile, MOFPED officers, with the support of consultants, intend to
complete this rigorous rationalization process, intime to use the results inthe preparation
o fthe budget for next fiscal year, and firmingup the MTEFfigures.

 104.     Because both civil service and pension reforms involve a significant amount o f
technical work, as well as consultations across a broad spectrum o f stakeholders, the
PRSC mechanism i s required to provide a consistent reinforcement to regular policy
dialogue and help keep the Government on track with the difficult reforms. However,
there i s need for stronger commitment and a clear road map from GOU to move forward
with this agenda.

PEAPStrategic Objective 4.4.4 -Strengthened local government system for service
delivery

105.      Decentralization o f service delivery inUganda has been ongoing for more than a
decade, guided by the 1993 Local Governments Statute and the 1997 Local Governments
Act. These acts consolidated and streamlined laws on decentralization and devolution o f
functions, powers, and services to local governments to ensure good governance and
democratic participation in, and control of, decision making by the people. Local
governments currently have the power to hire and fire staff, elect councilors through
universal adult suffrage, collect revenue from its own sources, and prepare development
plans and budgets that are approved by its council without any recourse to central
government.

106.      Bank involvement in decentralization reforms since 2000 has been through the
first and second Local Government Development Project (LGDP I& E), focusing on (i)
support to the Ministry o f Local Government and Local Government Finance
Commission, (ii)   provision o f discretionary development and capacity building grants to
local governments, and (iii)  support to enhance local government revenues.

107.     Achievements under PRSC6 included:

   (i)   The Government developed the Decentralization Policy Strategic Framework
         (DPSF) as a singlereference source for decentralization policy inUganda.

  (ii)   The Government developed the 10-year Local Government Sector Investment
         Plan (LoGSIP) as a single document to guide, coordinate, and harmonize
         interventions  by development partners and MDAs in supporting the


                                               48

        implementation o f the decentralization policy. The plan covers six pillars: (a)
        local service     delivery, (b) political decentralization,     (c) administrative
        decentralization, (d) fiscal decentralization, (e) good governance, and (f) local
        economic development.

        Alternative sources o f revenue for local governments were identified and
        approved by the Cabinet. It approved two new sources o f revenue for LGs -a
        local service tax to be levied on persons in gainful employment, and an LG tax
        collected from hotel visitors and remitted by hotel owners. The third tax, a local
        tax paid by motor vehicle owners was not approved because a corresponding tax
        (vehicle licenses) at central government was being abolished.

        MoFPED provided compensation o f Ush 45 billion to LGs for revenue losses
        related to the graduated tax.

        The MoLG has been restructured and the new structure approved by the Cabinet.
        The new MoLG will now have two Directorates (LG Administration and LG
        Inspection) and five Commissioners (District Inspection, Urban Inspection,
        District    Administration,      Local     Council     Development,  and    Urban
        Administration). MoFPED has provided the necessary certificate o f financial
        implications and in FY2007/08 some provisional budget has been provided to
        start the recruitment process. The new structure now brings the total number o f
        MoLG staff from 122 to 164.
        The prior actions for PRsC7 are based on satisfactory progress on core

                         PEAPPILLAR 5-HUMAN          DEVELOPMENT
PEAPStrategic Objective5.1 -Bettereducatedsociety

110.      The Government continues to prioritize education by providing about 30 percent
o f its discretionary recurrent budget to education, with more than 60 percent spent on
primaryeducation. The Net Enrolment Rate (NER)at the primarylevel increasedto about
92 percent with equal numbers o f boys and girls. Results o f the national Assessment of
Progress in Education indicate that learning competencies, although still low, are on a
steady positive trend. To improve quality o f learning, the Government continues to hire
additional teachers and improve their conditions o f service, construct more classrooms,
and is carefully implementing the road map for reviewing the primary curriculum,
including instruction in the mother tongue at the lower grades. In addition, customized
performance targets for head teachers and their deputies are being implemented to
improve school-level supervision and reduce teacher absenteeism. Specific attention i s
paid to girls and districts with poor indicators, however, survival rates to primary5 for the
post-UPEcohort have stagnated at about 50 percent, and dropout rates and repetition are
still high, threatening achievement o f the MDGs.

111.      To increase access to secondary education, the Government began to implement
a Plan for Universal Post-Primary Education and Training. The enrollment rate for
secondary education improved from 25 percent in 2006 to about 30 percent in2007 with
45.6 percent o f these being girls. The Government also continues to implement the
Strategic Plan for Higher Education, inparticular a stronger focus on teaching science and
technology at universities, and the national Council for Higher Education began to
enforce minimum requirements for delivery o f academic programs inpublic universities.
The proportion o f students enrolled in science-related disciplines increased from 23.3
percent in2006 to 28.6 percent in2007.

112.      Despite the progress recorded, several challenges remain. Gender disparities,
particularly at post-primary levels, stagnation in primary completion rates, regional
inequalities, and deliveryo f education inthe North.

113.      Analytical underpinnings. Several studies to address key challenges in the
sector include: (i)  teacher recruitment, deployment, and retention for both primary and
secondary schools; (ii) for money studies are undertaken on an annual basis and
                          value

(vi) Diagnostic Studyon Causes o fLow PrimaryEducationCompletion Rates.

set o fmonitoring indicators, quantitative targets, and key actions for education:

      Decline inthe pupil-teacher ratio from 55:1to 50:1;

ProgressduringPRSC7
115.     The Government has been attempting to improve primary education quality by
increasing the number o f teachers, textbooks, and classrooms; curriculum review to
reduce overload; instruction inthe mother tongue inthe first three grades; and improving
school-level supervision through customized performance targets for head teachers and
their deputies. Given the constrained resource envelope, actively pursuing efficiency
measures in both primary and post-primary education remains a key to progress. Some
progress has been registered inseveral areas:

     Good progress was made in implementation o f recommendations from
     trackinghalue for money studies in the education sector. Pending actions to
     facilitate direct transfer o f per capita funds to primary schools would require
     amendment o f the law, which i s beingdiscussed at the Cabinet level.

     Efforts to increase the number o f teachers on the payroll reached 128,262 (99.4
     percent o f the approved ceiling o f 129,000 teachers). However, the target o f 51:l
     was not met because the increase in the number o f pupils was much higher than
     anticipated. Therefore, the pupil teacher ratio will remain at 52:l until the primary
     teacher ceiling i s increased from 129,000 or other modalities such as double-shift
     teaching are put inplace.

     The pupil-classroomratio improvedfrom 85:1in2004/05 to 76:1in2006/07. There
     were also notable improvements in the pupil-classroom ratios in some o f the 10
     targeted districts in which classrooms were in short supply. However, progress in
     some o f the targeted districts was slow due to prioritization of construction of
     teachers' houses rather than additional classrooms. Creation o f new districts out o f

116.     Completion rates stagnated at around 50 percent during the period 2005 to 2007
despite actions implemented (Table 10). A diagnostic study into causes o f low
completion rates that was undertaken prior to the sector review identified these major
causes o f low completion rates: (i)high drop-out rates (about 7 percent); (ii)high
repetition rate (15 percent); (iii)inadequate school-level supervision; and (iv) inadequate
involvement of other stakeholders, including communities and religious institutions that
are the foundation bodies o f most primary schools. An action plan to address these areas
has been agreed, and actions will be on the ground before commencement o f the next
school year.

 PEAPStrategicObjective5.2 -HealthierUgandans

 119.      The current set o f reforms inthe health sector started in2000/01 with adoption o f
 the first Health Sector Strategic Plan (HSSP) (2000/01 to 2005/06), which coincidedwith
 the PRSC1, and later through the second Health Sector Strategic Plan (2006/07 to
 2010/11). The reforms aim to provide Ugandans with a minimum package o f cost-
 effective health care interventions targeting the major causes o f diseases through a Sector
 Wide Approach (SWAp). Priority focused on: (i)controlling major communicable
 diseases, notably HIV/AIDS, malaria, and tuberculosis; (ii)improving child health
 services for immunization, diarrhea, and respiratory tract infections; (iii)improving
reproductive health care; (iv) sanitation; (v) mobilizing additional financing for health;
 (vi) recruiting and better deployment o fhealth workers; (vii) strengthening public-private
collaboration; and (viii) strengtheningthe health care delivery system through creation o f
health sub-districts by upgrading selected health centers (HC lVs) to provide emergency
obstetric and surgical care and constructing selected health centers (HC as) in
underserved remote areas.

 120.     Under the Health SWAp, overall sector performance is measured against the
HSSP indicators and undertakings selected at the Joint Review Mission (JRM). Table 11
demonstrates modest but consistent improvement in sector performance across most
indicators, most marked during HSSP 1. Funding from Government o f Uganda revenue
and allocation to the PHC Conditional Grant increased duringthis period with per capita
expenditures on health risingfrom US$5.9 to US$ 10 between2000/01 and 2005/06. The
increased funding made it possible to (i)   upgrade over 150 H C I V s and construct 800 H C
IIs, increasing coverage o f the populationresidingwithin a 5 kilometer radius o f a facility
from 58 percent to 72 percent with emergency obstetric and surgical care more available;
(ii) increase government funding to the PNFPs from USh.3.7 billion to 17 billion; (iii)
double the number o f qualified health workers from 33 percent to 68 percent, and (iv)
triple per capita spending on essential drugs from US$0.50 to US$ 1.80.

121.      During the HSSP 2 period there has beennotable progress in the proportion o f
children under five-years-old receiving malaria treatment within 24 hours, coverage o f
insecticide treated bed nets, intermittent presumptive treatment o f pregnant mothers for
malaria, TB cure rate and several o f the HIV/AIDS related indicators, most notably ART
treatment. To date over 100,000 out o f 300,000 patients eligible for antiretrovirals
treatment are being treated. Poliomyelitis and guinea worm disease have been eliminated,
and measles, a major cause of mortality in children, is controlled with few outbreaks
reported. The improvements registered by these programs are a result o f increased
attention andresources from the Global Health Initiatives.

122.      The 2006 Demographic and Health Survey reported improvements in
several key health outcome indicators, a departure from the previous demographic
health surveys (Table 12). Total fertility rate and supervised deliveries, which for a long
time had shown no signs o f improvement, registered modest gains. Ifthese trends can be
sustained then Uganda may have limited prospects for meeting its PEAP and MDG
targets,

123.       Undertakings are the other important criteria used to assess sector performance.
They guide and facilitate reforms by building consensus for actions directed toward
specific bottlenecks in the critical path of specific reforms. For partners providing budget
support, satisfactory implementation o f undertakings i s seen as a gauge o f the
Government's commitment to reforms in the sector. At every JRM, eight to 12
undertakings are selected for review in the next JRM. While overall implementation o f
undertakings remains satisfactory, there have been cases where implementation was
delayed or failed to start. Many o f these cases involve undertakings that require broad
buy-inbeyond the Ministry o f Health. This issue was discussed. Instead o f only selecting
undertakings within the jurisdiction o f the Ministry o f Health, it was agreed that each
selected undertakingshould have a plano f action with means to verify implementation.

124.     The 2007 JRM reviewed the status of sector performance in 2006, discussed
main bottlenecks impeding sector performance, and agreed on a new set o f undertakings.
Despite improvements at the household level reflected inthe recently completed Uganda
Demographic and Health Survey (2006), an assessment o f overall health sector
performance basing on the eight Poverty Eradication Action Plan (PEAP) indicators and
the 2006 undertakings reveals a slowing o f sector perfonnance improvements over the
last year. Modest improvements were noted for deliveries in health facilities, drug
stockouts, and contraceptive use, but they fell below the targets for the year (Table 11and
12).

125.     Of the 10undertakings agreed at the JRM of 2006, four were fully achieved,
two were partially achieved, and four were not achieved. Of the four undertakings not
achieved, closing the salary gap between government jobs and Private-Not-For-Profit
(PNFP) providers was singled out as the most critical given its potential to impact
negatively on sector performance because the PNFPs serve about 30 percent o f the
population. The 2007 JRM agreed to carry forward the undertaking and advised the
MinistryofHealthto continueto engagethe Ministries ofPublic Service and ofFinance,
Planning and Economic Development over the matter. The other undertakings not
achieved -appraisal o f all projects with respect to HSSP, finalizing the comprehensive
plan, and assessmentof capacity o fthe National DrugAuthority -were carried forward.
The Ministryo f Healthwas advised to planand budget for their implementationon time.

126.     The JRM discussedthe major bottlenecks impedingsector performance:

    (i)Humanresourcesforhealth.Thesectorsuffersfromrapidturnoverofhealth
          workers and has difficulty attracting and retaining health workers, especially in
          remote areas and new districts. With the HRH Strategic Plan finalized, the
          Ministryof Health should expedite its implementation, giving special attention
          to attracting and retaining health workers to remote and rural areas, and
          improving overall personnel management in the sector. The Ministryo f Health



                                                   56

     should continue holding discussions with other relevant government agencies to
     close the PNFP salary gap.
(ii)FunctionalityofHealthCentreIV. Health Center N s were established to
     bring emergency surgical and obstetric care services closer to communities and
     coordinate delivery o f health services at the health sub-district level. Many
     Health Center Ns are still not providing the full range o f services even when
     equipment and the staff are in place for a variety o f reasons. As part o f the
     Government's planto make sure all the H C I V s become functional, the Ministry
     o f Health will support 30 additional Health Center I V s to become operational by
     next JRM and scale-up comprehensive emergency obstetric care in 70 H C Ns
     andbasic emergency obstetric care andin400 HC IIIs.

(iii)Logisticsmanagement for medicines and supplies. The JRM noted that poor
     logistics management i s hampering improved procurement planning for
     medicines and supplies. Over 84 percent o f National Medical Stores supplies,
     largely third-party commodities, were being stored in various warehouses with
     attendant gross inefficiencies. Storage facilities are inadequate at all levels. A
     task force was appointed to handle challenges brought about by third-party
     procurement. In addition, emphasis will be placed on ensuring regular
     availability o f contraceptives at the facility level.

(iv) Managing the budget process. The JRM urged the Ministry o f Health to
     strengthen the BFP process to include all major sources o f funding that are
    presently off-government planning and budgeting. The JRM noted the recent
     adoption o f Long-Term Institutional Arrangements to manage Global Fund
    grants and other donor funds and proposals to establish a Budget Unit in the
    Health Planning Department and to strengthen the Procurement Unit o f the
    Ministry of Health. These actions should help the Government establish an
    appropriate fiduciary system for the sector and plan and manage the large in-
    flows o f external funds. Duringthe next fiscal year, the M o H agreed to appraise
    all major projects and incorporate them into the Government planning and
    budgetprocess, and prepareone comprehensive procurement plan.

(v) Local government capacity. The JRM found several factors were limiting
    sector performance at the district level - limited multi-sectoral action for
    health, inadequate facilitation o f Village Health Teams (VHTs) and HealthUnit
    Management Committees (HUMCs), vertical operation o f major programs, and
    the proliferation o f districts. In many cases, local governments were found to
    undermine enforcement o f public health laws and ordinances that mainly deal
    with sanitation. The Ministry o f Health, in conjunction with the Ministry o f
    Local Government, should support local government enactment o f by-laws to
    increase enforcement o f the Public Health Act and other related laws aimed at
    improving environmental hygiene and sanitation and roll out implementation o f
    the Village Health Teams. The JRM advised government to roll out
    implementation o f the two roadmaps: for reduction o f maternal and neonatal
    mortality, and infant andchildhood mortality completedrecently.




                                          57

127.    As part of the October 2007 undertakings, four from 2006 were carried
forwardandseven new ones selected:

        Dialogue between the Ministryo f Health, the Ministry o f Finance, Planning and

         Systematically operationalize at least additional 30 H C I V s in an equitable

        Assess capacity o f the National DrugAuthority to optimally fulfill its mandate in

        Overall, the conclusion of the JRM is that despite a difficult year given the
Global Fund and GAVI scandals, the SWAP institutional arrangements for the
sector remain strong. However, the sector faces a number of risks. The Auditor
General's report highlighted a number o f shortcomings with the overall accounting
environment in the sector. While the M o H was open in sharingthe report, it is not clear
how the issues will be tackled when the report is discussed by Parliament. These and
other issues are expected to be addressed through the HSSP 11 Mid-Term Review
scheduled for 2008. Considering the slowing down o f sector performance and sub-



                                             58

 optimal implementation o f the undertakings, the appraisal mission rated overall sector
 performance last year as marginally satisfactory.

 129.     HIV/AIDS. The JRM received a copy of the Final National Strategic Plan
 (2006/07 to 2010/11). The plani s prioritized, costed, and its preparation was informed by
 a critical and detailed assessment o f the epidemic. The plan identifies the drivers o f the
 epidemic in Uganda and the Government i s to be commended for the detailed analysis.
 The Government is preparing an annual plan for HIV/AIDS which will go a long way to
rationalize and harmonize the various vertical HIV/AIDSinitiatives, including ensuring
 their alignment to the National Strategic Plan. With the development o f the annual plan,
the Uganda A I D S Commission needs to strengthen the Annual Joint Review for
HIV/AIDSto monitor the national HIV/AIDSresponse.

 130.     The National Strategic Plan evidently notes that while HIV incidence may not be
increasing inUganda, the number o f new HIV cases yearly is quite highand recommends
 stronger prevention efforts. The JRM endorsed this recommendation. Uganda is in a
 strong position for the first time to mount a vigorous national response. The resources
available during the first two half years o f the National Strategic Plan can finance the
high case scenario. For Uganda to maximize these resources, it will need to strengthen
overall coordination o f the HIV/AIDS National Strategic Planresponse. Although Global
FundRound 7 was successful, Ugandaneeds to start consideringhow it will finance the
last two years o f the National Strategic Plan because most o f the current funding
arrangements will have ended.

PEAPStrategic Objective 5.3 -Improved access to safe water and sanitation
services

 131.     The Government of Uganda continues to make modest progress toward
achieving PEAP and MDG targets on access to safe water,25as noted by the seventh
Joint Sector Review (JSR) which took place in October 2007. Against the PEAPtarget o f
77 percent (MDG target 62 percent) for rural water supplies, the sector recorded 63
percent in 2007. For urban water coverage, 71 percent access was achieved against the
PEAP target o f 77 percent (MDG 77 percent). This is a solid record o f performance
compared to the 55 percent rural access (2000) and 60 percent urban access (2002)
recorded when the Water and Sanitation Sector-Wide Approach to Planning (SWAP) was
just taking shape (Fig. 1). Functionality o fwater pointsz6stand at 83 percent inrural areas
and 82 percent in small towns. In2007, access to improved sanitation facilities27stood at
59 percent (against the MDGtarget o f 72 percent); the pupil: latrine ratio stood at 69:1as
o f June 2007 compared to 61:1inJune 2006 (against the target o f 40:1).

132.      The Annual Joint Sector Reviews have highlighted progress in the following
areas:

          About 80 small towns were gazetted as urban water and sewerage authorities
          and placed under a regime o f six cluster private sector management contracts.
          About 80 percent o f the towns are currently under private sector management
          usinglocal Ugandanoperators. Again, Uganda is clearly a leader inthis kindo f
