         internal management contracts;

         Sanitation recorded only a slight increase from 58 percent coverage last year to
         59 percent this year. Stronger progress i s inhibited by a lack o f attention to
         sanitation as a priority at all levels, financial constraints, insufficient human
         resources, inadequate dissemination o f best practices, poor urbanplanning, high
         investment costs, andpoor operation and maintenance o f sewerage.

133.    Key ingredients for success in the water and sanitation sector include (i)         a
genuine Government commitment to reforms, (ii)thorough sector analysis and
preparation, (iii)consensusbuildingbetween the Government and donors, (iv) centralized
technical assistance support (pooled resources) to take care o f complex and analytical
issues, and (v) systematic restructuringandcapacity buildingo f sector institutions.




                                               60

          These sector challengeswere identified:

           Need for increased efficiency. Unless there are concerted efforts to ensure
           enhanced efficiency, sustainability, and better targeting, the MDGREAP targets
           on increased coverage may be at risk, particularly given that the population i s
           growing at 3.4 percent per year.

           Delay in restructuringthe Directorate of Water Development (DWD). The
           long delay in filling staff positions risks falling back into the old discrete and
          multi-project management mode with a large number o f contract staff hired
          outside the organizational structure.

          Delegation. The Ministryo f Water andEnvironmentis keen to move ahead on
          delegation o f procurement functions and implementation o f the Integrated
          Financial Management System (IFMS), which is essential to build a sustainable
          institutionalplatform for the rational and efficient delivery o f services.

          Enhanced sector-wide regulatory framework. Such a framework should
          further strengthen monitoring and evaluation and therefore increase sector
          accountability and credibility. General periodic reporting on the part of DWD
          has improved, however, physical verification i s still a long way off.

          Increasedinfrastructurefor northern Uganda. The resettlement of refugees
          and return of Internally Displaced People (IDPs) in northern Uganda requires
          adequate infrastructure investments and operational capacity in rural areas and
          small towns. The sector has set aside additional resources for the north,
          however, the destruction o f infrastructure during recent floods i s creating new
          challenges. The sector's needs assessmenthas been elaborated inthe PRDP.

135.     Rising unit cost is constraining faster progress in increasing access to safe
water. The number o f additional people served annually is hardlymeeting the population
increase each year, and unit costs in the rural and small towns are increasing. For
example, unit cost per person served inrural areas rose from about Ush 16,000 to 27,000
between 2002/03 and 2006/07 (Fig. 2). The reasons include a marked reduction in the
availability o f cheap technologies, increased cost o f other resources as well as increases in
overhead costs as a result o f the creation o f new districts. The proportion o f water sector
grants available for water supply facilities dropped from 81percent to 70 percent over the
last five years (due to the set-up costs innew districts). Governance issues also cannot be
ruled out.

136.     Progress under PRSC7. The Joint Sector Review (September 2006) agreed on
six undertakings for PRSC7. The sector has substantially achieved the undertakings, with
the exception o f undertaking No. 6 on Water for Production (WfP) where the focus
deviated considerably from the original aim o f mobilizing private sector funding and
community contributions. The sector grant for rural water and sanitation increased byUsh
5 billion (from 40 billion in 2006/07 to 45 billion in 2007/08) to increase focus on the
poor. The extra amount was allocated to districts with coverage below 50 percent to
improve equity. The budget for WfP increased from Ush 5 billion in 2006/07 to Ush 15
billion in 2007/08. The budget for water supply and sanitation for internally displaced
people in the north increased from Ush 1.7 billion in2006/07 to 4.2 billion in2007/08 to
take care o f water and sanitation needs for resettlement areas. Under good governance,
key actions included implementation o f recommendations from VFWtracking studies
from 2004 and 2005 and setting up a feedback mechanism (information to bidders) aimed
at enhancing transparency in procurement and ultimately contribute to value for money.
The undertaking on improving financing mechanism for sanitation is intended to
gradually improve the sanitation and hygienecoverage.

CROSS-CUTTINGISSUES OF IMPORTANCE

137. Crosscutting issues were identified as part o f developing the P E N . Of these, the
PRSC has been supporting work inthe areas o f gender, population growth, and infant and
maternal mortality. The objective i s to help the Government formulate policies to address
these issues across sectors.



                                               62

 138. Gender. The gender-focused agenda o f PRSC7 consolidates the actions launched
under PRSC5 and focuses on some gender responsive actions required to achieve the
PEAP objectives. PRSC6 suggestedways o f broadening the agenda to gender and equity
to enhance inclusion. The actions so far include implementation o f the gender and equity
budget guidelines in five sectors, passage and implementation o f the pending bills, and
implementation o f the Gender and Growth Assessment recommendations. However,
overall progress on the actions has been mixed. The Ministry o f Gender, Labour and
 Social Development in collaboration with the National Integrated Monitoring and
Evaluation Strategy (NIMES) Secretariat prepareda Gender and Productivity Report in
June 2008.

 139. The GOU acknowledges that addressing issues o f active community participation
andgender inequalities indevelopment programs is critical for improvedservice delivery.
The Government underscores the need for a multi-pronged approach to address social
development concerns by providing technical support to the relevant sectors, capacity
building, for example, for gender mainstreaming, promoting adequate staffing at the local
government level, and creating opportunities for community improvement.

 140. While continuing to support the above gender responsive actions, PRSC7
encourages the GOU to consider strengthening the voices o f the vulnerable and the
demandside of service delivery through implementation o fthe Community Mobilization
and Empowerment Strategy (CMES), which has now been approved by the Cabinet. The
provision o f safety nets to the poor in order to help them access basic services, protect
them against risks and consequences o f livelihood shocks, and promote equity i s a
concern to the Government. Formulation o f a Social Protection Policy will go a long way
inproviding options for instruments to clarifyroles andresponsibilities.

141. Populationgrowthand infant and maternalmortality. Rapid population growth
creates a challenge to Uganda's poverty eradication efforts. The National Population and
Housing Census o f 2002 found the total population o f Uganda to be 24.4 million, an
increase o f 8 million since the last count in 1991, making Uganda the third fastest
growing country in the world with a rate o f growth o f 3.2 percent. At this growth rate,
Uganda's population was projected at over 27.6 million in 2005 and 39.3 million by
2015. By 2050, Uganda's population i s projectedto be 103.2 million, makingit the fourth
most populous nation in Sub-Saharan Africa (UN World Population Prospects, 2002).
Uganda also has the third highest fertility rate in the world - an average Ugandan
woman bears 6.9 children, and one o f highest dependency ratios in the world o f 1-11                   ,'*
Hightotal fertility, short birthintervals, limited access to contraceptive services, and low
age o f first pregnancy are the key drivers o f Uganda's highpopulation growth rate. Poor
and less educated households in Uganda experience higher fertility and higher
dependency ratios.




28. The international definition o f dependency ratio refers to the share o f the dependent population
(children aged 0-14 years and the elderly, age 65 and above) over of the total adult population (age 15-64
years, Le., potential wage earners).


                                                   63

 142. A discussion paper by MoFPED estimates that if no action i s taken to reduce
fertility, an additional 1.3 million Ugandans, approximately 28 percent o f the total
population, will live in poverty by 2013.''             It recognizes population growth as an
impediment to poverty eradication, a contributing factor to high maternal and infant
mortality, a hindrance to attaining education and employment objectives, and an
unsustainable pressure on natural resources. It identifies key determinants o f lower
fertility priority interventions as secondary education for girls and access to family
planning. The paper concludes that a "reduction inthe growth rate o f 1percentage point
could boost economic growth inthe medium term by 0.6-2.8 percentage points per year."
Klasen (2005) notes that if Uganda began a period o f sustained fertility decline, it could
boost its per capita income growth rates by between 0.5 and 0.6 percentage points per
year in the medium term, and the associated favorable age structure dynamics would
result in a 1.5 to 5 percent increase inper capita income growth per annum in the longer
term.

143. To address these challenges, MoFPED's Population Secretariat, together with the
M o H and a number o f NGOs, has developed a National Family Planning Advocacy
Strategy to spearhead and oversee the revitalization o f family planning services in the
country. Government has also initiated a review o f the population policy with a view to
incorporating recent findings and strengthening the advocacy role o f the Population
Secretariat, especially around family planning.

144. Moreover, an Infant and Maternal Mortality Task Force, including government
officials (from various ministries) and development partners, and chaired by the
Population Secretariat, was created to articulate the Government's strategy on the multi-
sectoral response to high infant mortality rate (IMR). This resulted in production o f an
Infant and Maternal Mortality Task Force Report3' calling for tackling IMR beyond the
health sector. The Task Force recommended focusing on the following interventions over
the next five to 10 years: universal access to effective primaryhealth package; improved
access to and quality o f Emergency Obstretic Care; expansion o f family planning
programs; intensification o f adolescent health interventions; and nutrition, together with
universal education for girls and gender empowerment at the household and community
levels. The task force concluded that although Uganda had enacted many relevant
policies, there was inadequate focus on the key elements that could accelerate reduction
o fmaternal andinfantmortality rates.

145. To facilitate coordination o f the multi-sectoral approach, it was agreed to
institutionalize task force fhctions within the Population Secretariat, which has
continued lobbying efforts inmultiple sectors and advocating for IMMRissues using new
tools. The Secretariat's key function i s advocacy via consultative discussions and selected
analytical work. Last year the Secretariat tracked resource flows for maternal mortality,
and early this year it presented findings tracking resources for infant and child
healtWmortality. Addressing these issues will require sustained and systematic


29. Population Growthand Poverty Eradication, Discussionpaper 9, MFPED, Dec. 2004.
30. Ministry o f Finance, Planning and Economic Development, 2003b, Infant and Maternal Mortality in
Uganda - Causes, Interventions and Strategy for the Way Forward, Kampala.


                                                   64

commitment over the long term, but the PEAP matrix and APIR process allow for
 assessing progress on an annual basis. It was also agreed during the last Health Sector
Joint Review Mission that the M o H would prepare road maps for maternal mortality and
child health, charting key actions (with timelines) for the relevant institutions. These two
documents will be used to monitor the Government's efforts to reduce child and maternal
mortality.


                       VI.     OPERATION IMPLEMENTATION

POVERTY AND SOCIAL IMPACT

 146.    Poverty andSocial Impact Assessments (PSIAs) have allowed the Government to
analyze the distributional impact o f policy reforms on the well-being o f different
stakeholder groups, with particular focus on the poor and vulnerable. Uganda has a long
tradition o f analysis o fpoverty and social impacts o fpolicies, andmost core diagnostic or
sector work addresses these issues even though the final product i s not presented as a
PSIA. A large number o f such studies have been undertaken by the Government, the
Bank, and other partners to analyze poverty and social impacts o f policies, including the
PSIAs on the abolition o f user fees in health clinics (2004) and the Strategic Exports
Program (2003). A comprehensive poverty assessment was undertaken in 2006 for the
period 1992/03 to 2002/03. A PSIA on lands innorthern Uganda was prepared jointly by
the Ministry o f Lands andthe World Bank.

 147.    A study entitled "Local Government Revenue Policies and their Impacts: Model
for Tanzania and Uganda" (June 2007) provides an overview o f past local taxation. The
model presented in the study will allow a more detailed PSIA that sheds light on the
impacts of different options for local revenue collection in two dimensions. The first o f
these i s the distributional impacts (taking into account both revenue and expenditure
effects). Options for local taxation tend to suffer from a contradictory dynamic. If the
measures considered are progressive (e.g., property taxation, taxation o f formal sector
transactions or incomes) then they tend to be ineffective at raising revenue in poor and
marginal districts. Ifrevenue can be raised at the local level inrural areas it also enhances
the chances that resources will be available for utilization at the parishlevel, which has
been starved o f funds since the abolition o f the graduated tax. It i s important therefore to
consider both the revenue and expenditure dimensions o f taxation options. The second
dimension will be the impacts of different revenue options on the accountability o f local
government to citizens, which is widely perceived as having suffered since the abolition
o fthe graduated tax.

148.     The assessmento fthe poverty and social impact o fthe measures proposed by the
PEAP and supported by the PRSC is carried out in the context o f Uganda's PEAP
monitoring system and inparticular the biannual household surveys that analyze, among
other things, poverty trends and dynamics. The surveys, which cover the entire country,
provide a rich source o f information on household socioeconomic characteristics,
including consumption expenditures, employment, and access to social services. The
surveys are complemented by the more qualitative participatory poverty assessments.



                                              65

 Emphasis i s increasingly placed on outcome in the PEAP monitoring and indicators that
provide direct information on the status o fpoverty inall its dimensions, including gender-
related issues (for example, a baseline assessment on women and land was recently
completed). Monitoring these indicators i s based on a broad range o f surveys and
administrative data systems. In2005/06, the Government supported by the World Bank
carried out "Moving Out o f Poverty" as part o f an international program to assess the
determinants for moving inand out poverty.

IMPLEMENTATION, MONITORING,AND EVALUATION

 149.    The Government, through the Office o f the Prime Minister (OPM), leads
implementation o f the PRSC-supported program. The Government-wide coordination o f
poverty reduction reforms i s managed at four levels: the Cabinet level through the Policy
Coordination Committee under the Prime Minister; the Permanent Secretary level under
the Head o f Public Service/Secretary to Cabinet; the technical level under the Permanent
Secretary in the Office o f the Prime Minister; and in sector working groups. The Prime
Minister's Office i s responsible for servicing these coordination arrangements. The PRSC
team has helped to work out the transition arrangements, and will continue to provide
support as appropriate. A sustainable solution will not only require budget allocations for
these functions, but also continued efforts by the Prime Minister's Office, to
operationalize the Cabinet decision to professionalize the functions. The programmatic
approach allows a continuous policy dialogue in close collaboration with other
development partners, consistent with the PEAP review mechanism and monitoring and
evaluation framework.

150.     The impact o f PRSC7 will be monitored within the context o f the overall PEAP
results and monitoring framework, arrangements for which are spelled out in Uganda's
National Integrated Monitoring and Evaluation Strategy (NLMES). At the national level,
the Government monitors development outcomes and impact to assess progress in
achieving PEAP objectives. Key monitoring mechanisms for poverty and sector-level
outcomes include regular household, service delivery, integrity, enterprise, demographic,
and health surveys. UBOS bears the prime responsibility for developing and
implementing the national (economic and socioeconomic) surveys. A national census and
survey calendar covering the period 2007 to 2012 has beenprepared at part o fthe Plan for
National Statistical Development. The PRSC team has helped prepare this survey
calendar and has made funding o f the agricultural census a priority under the rural prior
actions. The PRSC team will continue to support UBOS with adoption and
implementation o f the survey and census program and will follow-up closely the level o f
fundingfor outer year surveys. Inaddition, periodic participatorypoverty assessments and
other surveys are carried out. The results o f these surveys and other evaluations, and
further analysis o f development trends, are reflected in annual PEAP results progress
reports which will be the basis for the PEAP annual implementation review.

151.    Sector performance i s monitoredmainly through Sector Working Groups. For the
education, health, water and sanitation, and justice, law, and order sectors, GOU has
established a consistent set o f monitoring indicators and time-bound quantitative targets
for inputs and outputs that are tracked at regular intervals. To collect the needed data on



                                             66

indicators and to monitor progress in achieving the targets, the education, health, and
water and sanitation sectors have established management information systems that
collect information from each service point. The Plan for National Statistical
Development seeks to further strengthenthese M&E systems as well as to develop M&E
systems in all other sectors. Inaddition to the management information systems, all these
sectors conduct annual expenditure tracking surveys to monitor the flow o f funds, and
value-for-money studies have also become common. Biannually, the Government and
development partners assess the performance o f each sector, discuss emerging issues, and
agree on new undertakings and quantitative targets.

FIDUCIARYASPECTS
152.     Foreign exchange system. The Safeguards Assessment o f the Bank o f Uganda
(BoU), which was completed by the IMF on April 13,2003, identifiedboth strengths and
vulnerabilities o f BoU financial systems. The vulnerabilities, particularly with regard to
external and internal audits, have been mitigated through various measures taken by BoU.
The IMF Review Mission o f September 2006 that updated the Safeguards Assessment
noted that seven out o f 10 recommendations o f the 2003 safeguards recommendations
hadbeen fully implemented. To this effect, audited financial accounts are put before the
legislature within the statutory period o f three months after the end o f the financial year;
an Audit Committee (of the Board) has been appointed, and procedures have been put in
place to ensure that both internal and external audit findings/recommendations,as well as
follow-up actions taken, are appropriately communicated to the Governor and the Audit
Committee o f the Board.

153.     Furthermore, economic andfinancial data are now reconciled on a quarterly basis
and an audit plan assessing the risk o f BoU operations and the related control
environment i s created. Following the institution o f a risk management framework in the
year (2005), B o U conducts risk management reviews on a quaterly basis. The 2006
Assessment also noted additional challenges including: (i)   the delay inappointment o f the
board that could impair the ability o f the Board and the Audit Committee to fulfill its
functions; (ii)the inadequacy o f a formal mechanism to monitor and report on the
implementation o f external audit recommendations; and (iii)an unclear method to
determine distributable income because the unrealized gains and losses are not separately
tracked.

154.    Budget resource management system. The 2006 PFM performance report,
based on the Public Expenditure and Financial Accountability (PEFA) measurement
framework covering the period 2002/03 to 2004/05, notes marked improvements in the
reporting o f financial information in Uganda. It describes improved transparency and
availability o f actual expenditure data, quality and timeliness o f annual financial reports,
and periodic reconciliation. Progress i s also noted on the Country Integrated Fiduciary
Assessment (CIFA, 2004) action plan, particularly the rollout o f the IFMS, and activities
under the PPDA. Risk associated with lack o f clear rules and regulations has been
reduced through appropriate legislation and regulatory frameworks.




                                              67

 155.     Over the years, fiduciary risks related to poor budget formulation and budget
 preparation processes have beenreduced and progress will continue to be guided by the
 Public Expenditure Management Committee (PEMCOM) to coordinate PFM reforms
 now encapsulated in the FINMAP. The Government Financial Statements are now being
prepared in accordance with the International Public Sector Accounting Standards
 (IPSAS) framework. The timeliness and completeness o f reporting has improved while
training and capacity buildingfor staff in central and local governments both at technical
 andprofessional levels has continued and has seen a significant increase inthe number of
professionally qualified staff. The Auditor General has been provided access to the
 accounts on classified expenditures since FY2004.

 156.     However, the PFM Report also raises concerns about the existence o f unspent
balances in spite o f unmet commitments and that some government units continue to
 accumulate arrears in spite o f a stricter Commitment Control System. Audits o f central
government agencies continue to highlight weaknesses in the use and monitoring o f
resources by spending units. Inthe current 8thParliament, the Public Accounts Committee
and the Local Government Public Accounts Committee have become more active and
systematic infollowing up on irregularitiesidentified inthe Auditor General's Reports.

 157.    The decentralization o f activities to the local government level over the last 10
years means that 40 percent o f the total budgeted expenditures for spending agencies i s
now spent at that level. Seventy-five percent o f PAF resources also are channeled through
local governments. The Local Government Integrated Fiduciary Assessment (LGIFA)
benchmarking exercise o f a sample o f districts and municipalities, together with the
results o f a financial management study commissioned in 2003, assessed the state o f
public financial management at the local government level. The LGIFA highlights the
considerable progress made over the last decade in providing services at the local level;
from this base, however, it notes with concern that the budgeting and planningprocesses
o f local governments are poor at articulating specific local needs within overall national
objectives and policies. The assessment also raises concerns over the ability, desire, and
willingness o f local residents and politicians to hold their administrations to account for
their performance.

DISBURSEMENT AUDITING
                  AND

158.     It is proposed that the credit amount will be disbursed in two separate tranches.
The first tranche will be disbursed immediately once the credit becomes effective. The
second tranche will be disbursedonce the tranche release triggers outlined in paragraph
45 are satisfactorily fblfilled. The disbursements will follow IDA'S simplified
disbursement procedures for development policy lending and will be disbursed against
satisfactory   implementation    o f the    development    policy program.      Satisfactory
implementation will be indicated by prior actions and maintenance o f a satisfactory
macroeconomic policy framework, and not tied to any specific purchases or be subject to
procurement requirements. For each o f the two tranches, IDA will disburse the proceeds
into an account designated by the GOU that is part o f the country's foreign exchange
reserves accounts, acceptable to the Bank.




                                             68

 159.     The Borrower will ensure that upon deposit the foreign exchange amount will
immediately be converted to Uganda shillings and credited to the Uganda Government
Consolidated Fund Account, to be used subsequently for budgeted public expenditures.
The Borrower will acknowledge receipt to IDA o f the amount deposited in the foreign
currency account and credited in local currency to the Consolidated Fund Account. If,
after deposit inthe foreign reserve account, the proceeds o f the credit or any part thereof
are used for excluded expenditures, (Le., to finance goods or services on the standard
negative list) as defined in the Financing Agreement, IDA will require the Borrower to
either: (i) return that amount to the foreign reserve account for use for eligible purposes,
or (ii) rehnd the amount directly to IDA. Amounts refunded to IDA upon such request
shall be cancelled. The administration o f this credit will be the responsibility o f the
Ministry o fFinance.

 160.     The following summarizes the fiduciary assurance requirements for receipt o f the
credit:

  (9      Foreign reserve account. The GOU will acknowledge receipt to IDA that the
         money has been deposited in the foreign reserve account and the amount was
          credited in local currency to the GOU Consolidated Fund Account. While no
          audit i s required, it i s expected that confirmation o f receipt will be signed off by
         both the Accountant General and the Auditor General. IDA reserves the right to
         request an audit o fthe account as provided for inthe financing agreement.

  (ii) Public (government) accounts. The Auditor General is requiredby law to
         produce hisker annual report to Parliament on the public accounts within nine
         months o f the financial year-end. IDA will have access to those audited accounts.
  (iii) BankofUganda.TheannualentityfinancialstatementsoftheBankofUganda,
         auditedinaccordance with international auditing standards as promulgatedbythe
         InternationalFederation o fAccountants, will be publicly available.
ENVIRONMENTAL         ASPECTS

161,     The Bank has determined that the policies supported by the proposed PRSC7 are
not likely to cause significant effects on Uganda's environment and natural resources.
The potential environmental and social impacts o f the PRSC are not expected to result in
major adverse, or irreversible, environmental impacts. Biophysical environmental
impacts, thought to be modest given the nature o f the activities, can be foreseen at this
time only ina generic manner. The potential for landacquisition, resettlement, and effects
on cultural resources also cannot be ruled out, but such effects are likely to be limited in
extent given the de-emphasis on large-scale construction in the PEAP approach and the
nature o f IDA support.

162.     The environmental impacts o f activities in agriculture, education, health, and
water and sanitation are expected to be sufficiently addressed by appropriate Ugandan
institutions and implementing agencies. Issues over land use, land tenure, and common
property may arise in some o f these sectors. IDA currently has a strong presence in these
sectors and will continue to work with counterparts to ensure that appropriate mitigating
measures are promoted and incorporated into the gradcredit activities. Table 14 shows

 R I S K S AND RISK MITIGATION

 163.       Risks are briefly discussed below with a particular emphasis on those that are
more relevant for the PRSC program and PRSC efforts to mitigate them. In addition,
 specific risks to implementation o f core areas o fthe program are also identified.

Risksthat reflectthe broadercontext inUganda

 164.       Policies in support of private sector development. Currently, there is a need
 for some clarity on how programs such as Rural Development Strategy, Model Sub-
county Development Program, and Microcredit Outreach relate to the existing programs.
 Some interventions which subsidize specific investors (e.g. provision o f government
land) may deviate from the free market principles embodied in the PEAP. There have
been concerns expressed by Parliament about direct subsidies or incentives offered to a
number o f businesses. The Bank has engaged the Government in high-level policy
dialogue and through PRSC discussions to minimize such interventions. The Bank's
advice has been that government-led initiatives should benefit businesses as a whole
rather than be targeted to a particular firm, and that they should be tried on a pilot basis
before large public funds are committed. A strong and stable government apparatus, with
transparent and consistent decision making and priority setting processes, will help
mitigate this type o frisks.

 165.       Management of oil revenues. Uganda has recently found economically viable
quantities o f high grade oil in the west, leading to accelerated exploration efforts and
discussion on revenue sharing arrangements. The National Oil and Gas Policy has been
developed to ensure transparency and to prevent waste and corruption in the use o f oil
revenues. As there i s some concern by local governments and residents that oil revenues
will not benefit them, the management of possible large scale oil revenues will remain a
sensitive issue for regional peace and for sharing benefits internally. The Bank i s offering
technical advice through the Extractive Industries Transparency Initiative to promote
prudent oil revenue management and mitigation against undesired economic
consequences.

166.       Governance challenges. Although the Government has made some progress in
highlightingcorruption involving use o f public funds, the investigation and prosecution
has been slow and under-resourced. Corruption threatens public confidence, wastes
scarce resources, and reduces the impact o f government programs. The Bank i s working
with the Government and development partners to support a more vigorous
implementation o f Uganda's national anti-corruption action plan. The PRSC dialogue has
been broadened to tackle corruption through a multi-pronged approach o f enhancing
accountability (PFM and procurement), programming for better value for money (sector
reviews), and facilitating enforcement (case backlog management, special anti-corruption
court).

167.       Institutionaland public sector capacity is weak, particularly at sub-national
and facilities levels. Uganda needs to address public sector weaknesses arising from
inadequate incentive mechanisms and weak human resources planning, monitoring and


                                              71

 supervision mechanisms. Strong ownership o f the PEAP agenda by the entire public
 sector and the civil society, and ongoing public sector reforms, should contribute to
 sustained improvements in public service capacity. A comprehensive pay reform is
currently not possible due to limited resources and an already high share o f employee
costs in the budget. The Bank is working with a government task force on various
 scenarios for designing a viable public sector reform which include right-sizing o f civil
 service, implementing an agreed pay policy and cost efficiency and effectiveness in the
use o f humanresources. Inaddition, the Government needs to strengthen humanresource
planning, performance management and enforcement o f disciplinary measures inorder to
improve productivity andaccountability for service delivery.

 168.    Exogenous shocks, arising from adverse weather patterns or global economic
slowdown, can adversely affect the Ugandan economy. Recent drought reduced
hydroelectric power production to below half o f capacity and stalled the growth o f
agricultural production. Reliance on thermal power generators at current oil price trends
will prolong emergency budgetary resources allocated to meet growing electricity
demand. Impatiencewith the current crisis i s givingrise to those who think that fiscal and
monetary policies have been too conservative. The Bank's Country Economic
Memorandum stresses that the emphasis placed on pursuing sound macroeconomic
policies, structural and institutional reforms, and improving access to markets, was
essential in sustaining growth and enhancing competitiveness. The PRSC continues to
support efforts to enhance production, competitiveness, and incomes o f the poor who are
the most vulnerable to these shocks.

Risksspecific to the PRSC

169.     Growth of the Public Administration budget. As noted in relation to PRSC5
and 6, the growth o f the public administration budget remains a challenge for PRSC7.
Expenditures related to the referendum on constitutional amendments in2005, holding o f
the national elections in2006, and hosting the CHOGM in 2007, were among the factors
contributing to growth o f Public Administration expenditures. These one-off events had
the effect o f competing for resources with priority service delivery. The Bank i s
monitoring the budget closely with a view to mitigate the impact o f these one-off events
to the service delivery sectors andwill assess the composition o f the budget as part o f its
assessmento fthe triggers for the release o f the second tranche o fPRSC7.

170.     Accountability on the use of public funds. Budget discipline needs to be
maintained and competing budget priorities balanced through the existing consultative
process. Supplementary allocations need to be limited to emergencies. Public
procurement relies on open competitive bidding but in some cases the provisions for
waiver o f competitive bidding have been used. The Bank undertook a PFMibudget
reporting assessment in parallel with the preparation o f PRSC7 whose prior actions are
focusing more on strengthening budget reporting, auditing, and compliance with the
procurement law.

171.     Sustainable service delivery. Capacity and resource constraints in key social
service sectors such as education and health may undermine the Government's efforts on



                                             72

quality improvement. The demographic factors point to potential unsustainability o f
current programs, and thus, new programs will need to be considered carefully with
population pressure duly considered. The 2007 PER highlights structural mismatches in
teacher deployment, as well as high teacher absenteeism. The ongoing 2008 PER i s
looking at ways to improve efficiency inthe health andagriculture sectors.

Dear Mr.McIntire,

PRSC 7 LETTER OF DEVELOPMENT POLICY

1. Iam writing to request, on behalf of the Government of the Republic of Uganda, for
the Seventh Poverty Reduction Support Credit (PRSC7) of USD 200 million in support of
our strategy under the Third Poverty Eradication Action Plan (PEAP) during the hnancial
year 2007/08 and 2008/09. T h i s letter sets out the actions that Government will undertake
over the medium term to implement the strategy, focusing on measures that will increase
the efficiency and effectiveness of Government operations in promoting growth and
reducing poverty.

2.    The actions and outputs that Government agreed to undertake are highlighted in the
PEAP Policy and Results Matrix. The actions and outputs considered critical to the success
of our reforms have been implemented and completed in the prior actions for PRSC7 as
outlrned in section 11of this Letter of Development Policy.

Backgroundand RecentDevelopments

3.    The overriding objective of Government policy i s the eradication of poverty in
Uganda. Uganda's national comprehensive strategy for fighting poverty, the Poverty
Eradication Action Plan (PEAP) launched in 1997 and revised in 2000 and 2004, has
continued to guide Government policy. The PEAPhas been extremely influential inq d m g
the undertaking of sector-wide approaches to policy reform and investment programming,
and improving the focus of Government's three-year rolling Medium Term Expendture
Framework (MTEF) in the fight against poverty.




                                                74

 4.    The PEAPwas revised in 2004 through a highly participatory and consultative process
 that involved all key stakeholders including government ministries, academia, civil society
 and development partners. The revised PEAP i s structured around five overarching pillars
 namely: Economic management; Enhancing Production, competitiveness and incomes;
 Security, confict resolution and disaster management; Good governance; and Human
 development. The PEAP i s currently being revised and transformed into a five year
National Development Plan. The revision and subsequently implementation of the new
 national development plan will be informed by an independent evaluation of the PEAP
implementationduring 1997-2007.


Macroeconomic performance:

 5.    Uganda's economic growth performance remains strong, thanks to the sound pro-
market macroeconomic management. In FY2006/7 the economy grew at 6.7%, which was
a percentage point higher than the average for the previous five years. This performance
was driven by strong growth in transport and communications, construction, mining and
quarrying, and trade services sectors, which registered double-digit growth rates. The
outlook for FY2007/8 through FY2009/10 remains positive - growth is projected at 7.1%
in FY2007/08 and 7.0% for the remainder of the period. Inflation has been contained at
about 5% since 1995, albeit with periodic fluctuations arising from variations in agricultural
production and international oil prices. In FY2006/7, headline inflation rose moderately to
6.8% and underlying inflation to 7.8%, as a combination of higher world oil prices and an
unanticipated increase in domestic demand affected the general price level. Credit to the
private sector as a share of GDP stood at 9.2% in FY2006/7 and i s projected to increase to
9.5% inFY2007/8 and 11%by FY2009/10.

Trends inpoverty levels:

6. Consistent with the growth in the national economy, poverty levels have continued to
decline since 1992. The latest household survey conducted in FY2005/6 shows poverty to
have declined to 31%, from 38% in 2002/3 and 56% in 1992/3. The decline in poverty,
which was mainly driven by increases inincomes and improved distribution, was acheved
in all regions of the country and more so in the rural areas. Inequality of income declined
from 0.43 on the Gini Coefficient to 0.41, suggesting improved re-distribution of incomes
and access to essential services by the population.


7.   To strengthen the reform program and ensure sustainable results, the Government's
focus i s to improve both the efficiency of its delivery of public services and the
effectiveness of these services in reducing poverty. While these concepts are interlinked,
increased efficiency implies improved management systems and practices in the public
sector, including improved financial accountability. Improved effectiveness implies a closer
alignment between outcomes and policy objectives throughgreater focus on service outputs
during budget preparation and implementation. Government is therefore requesting the
Seventh Poverty Reduction Support Credit (PRSC7) from the IDA to support
Government's reforms in these areas and help finance implementation of the PEAP. The
reform programme outlined below will help to ensure the success of the PEAP. To hasten
alignment of PRSC with the national budget cycle, and for Uganda to have predictability in
resource flows, Government has proceeded with implementing the agreed reform program
that i s requested to be supported by PRSC7. Great care has been taken to ensure that the



                                              75

medium-term program i s consistent with the PEAP, through continued consultations
within Government andwith other Stakeholders.

8. The main challenge to Uganda's growth is the current energy shortage, which is
constraining industrial growth and investment ingeneral through increased operating costs.
The Government, with the support of the IDA, i s implementing a strategy to mitigate the
effects of reduced power supply in the short- and medium term. The strategy includes
increasing thermal energy production through public-private partnerships,          demand
management by supplying free energy saving bulbs, and in the medium-term, through the
development of the Bujagali hydroelectricity power plant.

 The Reform Program

9.   Government i s in the process of revising the PEAP into a National Development Plan
(NDP) which focuses on achieving faster poverty reduction on the basis of a strong and
sustainable economic growth.       It is hoped that the new 5-year NDP wdl become
operational with effect fromJuly 2008. The other initiative i s the Northern Uganda Peace,
Recovery and Development Plan (PRDP) to stabilise and recover the North in the next
three years through a set of coherent programmes in one framework that all stakeholders
will adopt.


10.     The selected policy reform measures set out below are grouped under the five
Pillars of the PEAP. The specific and monitorable actions, that Government has proposed
to undertake inkey reform areas prior to the presentation of the PRSC7 to the WorldBank
Board, have largely been implemented successfully despite delays experienced in several
areas due to other immediate priorities such as the Commonwealth Heads of Government
Meetings. These are benchmarks that are seen as critical for progress and implementation of
the reform program. In the areas where progress was deemed less than satisfactory, specific
remedial measures have been agreed with the PRSC Team. The Results and Policy Matrix
breaks down the policy actions that are contained in the PEAP Results and Policy Matrix
into annual policy benchmarks. This basically involved developing annual policy
benchmarks as series of policy components sequenced over the whole PEAP period to
achieve the agreed PEAP outcomes. W e have in addition identified areas of a cross cutting
nature that will need to be addressed if the sectors are to perform to their expected levels
and proposed strategies to dealing with those cross cutting areas.


11.     During the preparation of PRSC 7, the Government made good progress in the
following areas:

11.1Pillar 1: Economic management The undertaking inthis pillar focuses on budget
preparation and execution with the aim of ensuring equitable use of public resources and
promoting growth. The prior action agreed to was: In the annual Public Expenditure
Review (PER), Government agreed with Development partners on the Medium Term
Expenditure Framework (MTEF) for 2007/08-2009/ 10, and executed the FY2006/07
budget consistent with the budget allocation as appropriated and adjusted with approval of
Parliament. The prior action sought to (i) excessive growth of Government's overall
                                            limit
expenditure to achieve a medium-term fiscal deficit target of 6.5% of GDP; (ii)   maintain
poverty focus of public expenditure by ensuring that Poverty Action Fund (PA9
expenditures perform at 95% or more; and (iii) growth of expenditures by the security
                                                limit



                                              76

and the public administration sectors so that increasingly larger share of the budget can be
directed to priority spending. Although expenditure overruns were experienced again in
Public administration and defence due to unavoidable circumstances, the government
remains committed to reducing the share of these expenditures in the budget. Starting in
FY2006/07, these objectives were to be assessed using actual expenditure data for central
government spending units,

11.2 Pillar 2: Enhancing Production, competitiveness and incomes: The undertakings
in this pillar focus on improving and then sustaining a good investment climate and
promotinggrowthinthe agriculture sector. The agreed prior actions include:

   i. Prioritization and agreement on key undertakings in investment climate and
        competitiveness, inter-alia, the Companies bill, the Insolvency bill and the Policy
        Paper on the regulation of Pension sector. The companies bill and insolvency bill
        are inadvanced stages for submission to Cabinet and Parliament for approval. T h i s
        i s expected before end of this financial year. The pension paper was approved by
        Cabinet.


        The Government recognizes that improving the investment climate, in particular
        entry and exit mechanism, and reducing the cost of doing business in Uganda will
        enable more formal participation in the economy and the creation of formal sector
        jobs. Leveraging private sector capacity, both finance and technical capacity, will
        allow Government to transform the economy and eliminate poverty.


  ii. Satisfactoryimplementationofcoreundertakingsasagreedinthe2006JointPMA
        Annual Review by the 2007 Review, focusing on improved resource utilization and
        allocation to the agricultural sector, Water for Agricultural Production strategy, and
        establishment of an Agricultural Market Information System. The strategy for water
        for agricultural production has been delayed to September 2008 to broaden the
        scope of the background field work ina regionally balanced manner.

 11.3 Pillar 4: Good governance: Undertakings inthis pillar aim at promotingan efficient
 and motivated public sector, fight corruption and improve governance and strengthening
 local government capacity to provide decentralised services. The undertakings also aim at
 promotingaccountability inthe use of public resources. The agreed prior actions include:

  i. Satisfactory implementation ofundertakings drawn fromthe recommendations of
        the Public Financial Management Performance Report issued inJuly 2006, including
        budget reporting, payroll cleaning, financial reporting by parastatals, and timely
       issuance of Treasury Memoranda.


  ii. Satisfactoryimplementationandcompliancebyallpublicprocuringentitieswiththe
       Public Procurement and Disposal of Public Assets Act (2003) and its regulations. In
       this case, Government will ensure that the Public Procurement L a w and its
       regulations are revised appropriately to seal any loopholes, that all procuring entities
       have established and made operational Contracts committees and Procurement and
       Disposal Units, that PPDA carries out procurement audits on an adequate number
       of public contracts (not less than 200 selected from a wide range of procuring
       entities) and that appropriate action i s taken where non-compliance i s identified.



                                               77

        The revision of the PPDA Act and Regulations proved to be more complex given
        multiple issues and viewpoints that needed to be harmonised. The Government has
        reinforced the Task Force working on the revision and intend to submit the revised
        law to Cabinet and Parliament by September 2008.

 ...
 m.     Satisfactory implementation of undertakings agreed by the joint government-
        development partners' review of the National Anti-CorruptionAction Plan (NACS)
        for 2004-07. Support for the fight against corruption continues to be a priority with
        additional focus on improving synergies among accountability agencies and
        empowering the civil society in its monitoring and advocacy roles.           Priorities
       include: continuation of the development and review of the legislation to fight
        corruption; development of a new NACS, operationalization of the Anti Corruption
       Division of the High Court, enhanced funding of the anti-corruption agencies and
        strengthened capacity of civil society to support the demand side of accountability.
       Inaddition to this, the Governmentwillconduct the thirdNational Integrity Survey
       to measure the incidence of corruption and analyze the trends in the fight against
       corruption since the 2002 survey. T h i s will inform future strategies looking at the
       preventive side inthe fight against corruption.

ii~.Satisfactory progress inthe implementation of the new phase of Public Service
       Reform Program (PSRP) as evidenced by accomplishment of the undertakings
       agreed in the Joint Government and Development Partners consultative process of
       November 2006. The agreed undertakings include interventions that will seek to:
       enhance the performance and accountability of public service organizations;
       increase efficiency and ensure affordability of public service; attract and retain
       adequate number of skilled and able personnel in the public service; provide the
       public service with an enabling and empowering work environment; motivate and
       adequately remunerate public servants; and develop leadership and management
       skills throughout the public service. In addition, the Government will take specific
       measures to sanction those responsible for ghost workers in the payroll following
       the 2006 payroll cleaning exercise; (ii)    right-size and control growth in the public
       service, and (iii)  work towards achieving medium-term salary targets set in the
       August 2006 public service pay reformpolicy.

 v.    Satisfactory progress on core undertakings agreed by JARD 2006 reviewed by
       November 2007 duringJARD 2007, including: follow-up and full implementation
       of the partially completed prior actions under PRSC6. Under h s prior action
       particular focus i s given to the viability of Local Governments; Local Governments
       staffing, wage bill and pension arrears; the need for organized urban development
       so as to improve the provision of basic urban services; and monitoring of Local
       Government expenditures of central government transfers so as to ensure
       consistency with PEAP objectives.

11.4 Pillar 5: Humandevelopment:Prior actions inthis pillar are derived fromthe sector
undertakmg agreed between Government and the development partners during the annual
sector reviews. The undertakings aim to promote increased access, efficiency and quality of
services provided by the sectors. The agreed prior actions include:

i. Satisfactoryimplementation of the undertakings agreed inthe education sector


                                               78

      review inNovember 2006 and confirmed by the October 2007 review. Government
      has continued with efforts to improve primary learning outcomes and completion
      rates among other priorities. The prior actions include reducing pupil-teacher ratio to
       52:l and pupil-classroom ratio to 76:l nationally and by district, improvingprimary
       completion rates. Other critical indicators for which actions continued to be tracked
      include; percentage of pupils achieving defined levels of competency in literacy and
      numeracy; share of female students enrolled in post primary education and training
      institutions; and share of female students enrolled in tertiary institutions. Particular
       emphasis i s being put on removing constraints to girl's education. Government i s
       also implementing the road map for primary education curriculum review, and
       development and implementation of a road map for the review of the primary
       teacher   education (PTE) curriculum continue to be key actions               towards
      improvement of the quality of primary education.

 ii. Satisfactoryimplementationoftheundertakingsagreedinthehealthsectorreviewin
      November 2006 and confirmed by the 2007 review. Among the undertakings yet to
      be acheved closing the salary gap between the government and Private-Not-For-
      Profit (PNFP) Providers is one of the most critical for sector performance, as the
      PNFPs serve about 30% of the population. Renewed efforts will also be needed to
      achieve other priority actions such as appraisal of all projects with respect to HSSP
      and finalizing the comprehensive plan and assessment of capacity of the National
      Drug Authority. The 2007 review also agreed on new undertakings including:
      operationalizing Health Center I V s and scaling up emergency obstetric care;
      strengthening drug procurement and logistics management through implementation
      of a rolling procurement plan; strengthening the budget process with regard to
      Global Health Initiatives and establishing long-term institutional arrangements for
      the management of the Global Fundgrants in the country; and undertaking a study
      to assess efficiency of public spending inthe health sector as part of the PER2008.

 ...
 1~1. Satisfactory implementation of undertakings agreed in water and sanitation sector
      review in September 2006 and confirmed by the 2007 review with particular
      emphasis on a few areas. To address the high demand on water resources and
      conflicting uses, Government undertook to prepare and test a framework for
      participatory integrated water resources management. The issue of limited funding
      for sanitation has been addressed by conducting a study to investigate the best way to
      introduce an integrated sanitation and hygiene budget linelgrant to Local
      Governments.       The   development    and implementation       of frameworks      for
      Procurement and Contract Management Quality Assurance for service delivery i s
      expected to lead to a reduction in investment costs as well as operation &
      maintenance costs. In order to ensure equity and transparency, fund allocation for
      2007/08 is based on the Strategic Sector Investment Model to all sub sectors,
      ensuring increased funding to the rural sub sector, the post conflict districts and
      districts with low coverage.


12.     Progress on the detailed implementation of the reform programme has continued to
be evaluated under the specific sector reviews. Discussions within the sectors and with the
technical staff of the PRSC Team have been conducted on each of the above undertakings
to ascertain the status, and to agree on remedial measures where progress was deemed less
than satisfactory. Confirmation of progress ineach sector i s contained inthe submissions by



                                             79

the respective Ministries to the PRSC7 Team, and additional detailed reports on the
outcomes of the sector reviews can be availed on request.


Disbursement arrangements

 13.     The government i s committed to fulwling the prior actions under the PRSC7 which
were delayed due to capacity constraints and owing to re-allocation of Government
resources in hosting CHOGM in 2007.             Indeed, many of the reforms that required
legislative approval have resumed and are expected to be completed early in financial year
2008/09.     W e propose that the second tranche financing under PRSC7 expected for
financial year 2008/09 i s linked to the followingtriggers:
    (i) maintenanceofastablemacroeconomicenvironment;
    (ii) approvalofthebudgetforfinancialyear2008/09 whichisconsistentwiththe
             Government's PEAPand medium-term growth objectives; and
    (iii)    submission to Cabinet of draft amendments to PPDA Act and regulations.
             These triggers are expected to be completed by September 30,2008.

PEAPCoordinationand Results-Orientation

 14.     In the context of NIMES, Government has developed PEAP Implementation
review mechanism based on PEAP Results and Policy Matrix. This PEAP review will also
be a channel of dialogue between Government, Private Sector, C i d Society, and
Development Partners. The objective of the review mechanism i s to ensure that the PEAP
M&E system is used to feed into the key policy decision processes and planning for the
subsequent fiscal year.


15.      Apart from facilitating the reform process of Government, the Results and Policy
Matrix i s a key i n s m e n t inenhancing Government coordination mechanisms. T h i s will
enhance the understanding of what results or outcomes Government wouldwish to achieve
throughblocks ofpolicy actions that facilitate the achievement of particular outcomes. The
PEAPreview mechanism will indicate our performance towards achieving these outcomes
and any adjustments required inthe proceeding years.



16.      In this regard, the availabdity of timely statistics is critical, and Government has
been making steady progress toward this objective through the preparation of the Plan for
National Statistics Development and an increase in the core funding for Uganda Bureau of
Statistics through budget. In addition, Government i s requesting donors to set up a pooled
funding mechanism to support additional data collection activities - such as the annual
panel survey.


Conclusion

17.      IwouldhketotakethisopportunitytoreiterateGovernment'scommitmenttothe
implementation of the Poverty Eradication Action Plan. The actions to be undertaken over
the medium term under each PEAP pillar focus on measures that will improve, through
greater efficiency and effectiveness, both the quantity and quality of service delivery,
particularly to the poor. Government i s also committed to and has already embarked on




                                              80

plans to increase energy supply in order to ameliorate the negative impacts on industrial
production because of the energy gaps.


18.     Increased efficiency of service delivery implies improved management systems and
practices in the public sector, including improved financial accountability. Improved
effectiveness of service delivery requires closer alignment between outcomes and policy
objectives through greater focus on service outputs during budget preparation. The
requested financing i s expected to support the critical Government reforms set out in this
letter and to enable achievement of those objectives.


19.      On behalf of the Government of Uganda, Iwish to thank the IDA for the
assistance already provided under the six previous PRSCs and other instruments. They have
been critical in achieving a substantial reduction in poverty since the 1990s. Itrust that this
request for additional financing of USD200 million to be provided in two equal tranches as
budget support for frnancial years 2007/08 and 2008/9 through the proposed PRSC7 will
receive your favourable consideration.


Yours sincerely,

ANNEX3: FUNDRELATIONS          NOTE

Press Release No. 07/299
December 19,2007
International Monetary Fund

IMFExecutiveBoardCompletesSecondReviewUnderPolicySupportInstrument
(PSI) for Uganda

The Executive Board o f the International Monetary Fund (IMF) today completed the
second review under a three-year Policy Support Instrument (PSI) for Uganda. The PSI
was approved on December 15, 2006 (see Press Release No. 06/281). The program
objectives included macroeconomic stability, debt sustainability,          financial sector
deepening, and improved public sector financial management.

The Executive Board also approved Uganda's request for waiver for the non-observance
o f the endJune 2007 quantitative assessment criterion on the ceiling on the increase in
base money liabilities o f the Bank o f Uganda as well as for the subsequent modification
o f quantitative assessment criteria designed to limit such occurrences inthe future.

The IMF's framework for PSISis designed for low-income countries that may not need
IMF financial assistance, but still seek close cooperation with the IMFinpreparation and
endorsement o f their policy frameworks. PSI-supported programs are based on country-
owned poverty reduction strategies adopted in a participatory process involving civil
society and development partners. This i s intendedto ensure that PSI-supported programs
are consistent with a comprehensive framework for macroeconomic, structural and social
policies to foster growth and reduce poverty. Members' performance under a PSI is
reviewed semi-annually, irrespective o f the status o f the program (see Public Information
Notice No. 05/145).

Following the Executive Board's discussion, Mr. Takatoshi Kato, Deputy managing
Director and Acting Chair, stated:

"Uganda's economy has continued to perform strongly, owing to appropriate fiscal and
monetary policies and the country's structural reform efforts. A positive economic
outlook, however, hinges on addressing key infrastructure bottlenecks, financial sector
development, and maintaining sound economic policies in the face o f volatile foreign
exchange flows and budgetary pressures.

"A continued focus on price stability as the primary objective o f monetary policy has
allowed inflation to ease after temporary supply shocks last year. This focus will remain
essential as increased volatility in foreign exchange flows poses challenges to the conduct
o f monetarypolicy.




                                              95

"The government's restraint on less-essential current expenditure and continued efforts in
tax revenue collection will be vital to raise public saving, provide room for poverty-
reducing spending, and avoid domestic arrears. Expenditures associated with the
Commonwealth Heads o f Government Meeting in Kampala, floods, and peace in the
North have already led to some expenditure overruns this fiscal year, and lef limited
space to accommodate additional budgetary pressures.

"A strong focus on public investment is needed to eliminate infrastructure bottlenecks
and increase productivity. Implementation delays indonor-financed projects have ledthe
government to explore alternative approaches to infrastructure investment and financing.
Projects should be evaluated carefully in terms o f their cost effectiveness and impact on
debt sustainability, especially to the extent that financing maybe non-concessional.

"Transparency and an appropriate balance between spending and saving will be key
elements to maximize Uganda's benefits from future oil production. The government's
comprehensive approach to the management o f oil resources and the plans to introduce a
national energy policy are encouraging steps toward these objectives," Mr.Kat0 said.

ANNEX9:UGANDA SUSTAINABILITY
                          DEBT                         FRAMEWORK

                                INTERNATIONAL MONETARY FUNDAND
                          INTERNATIONAL DEVELOPMENT ASSOCIATION


                                                      UGANDA

                       Joint IMFWorldBankDebtSustainabilityAnalysis 31



                      Preparedbythe Staffs of the InternationalMonetary Fundand
                                   the InternationalDevelopmentAssociation


                             Approved byRobert Corker and Mark Plant (IMF)


                                    and Sudhir Shetty andBrian Pinto (IDA)

                                               November 28,2007



Based on thejoint Low-Income Country Debt Sustainability Framework of the World Bank
and the IMF, Uganda is assessed to be at low risk of debt distress. Its debt ratios have
improved substantially over thepast few years (thanks to HIPC and MDRI debt reliefl and
areprojected to do so over the medium term and beyond under the baseline scenario. Having
achieved debt sustainability, the authorities plan to address infrastructure constraints aiming
at reaching high economic growth. TheJoint DSA assesses the challenges and trade-o,fs of
increasingpublic investment whilemaintaining debt sustainability.


1.       Ugandahas achieveddebt sustainabilityby implementingsound macroeconomic
policiesand receivingdebt relief.The HIPC andMDRIdebt reliefhave improved Uganda's
debt sustainability outlook substantially by leading to a drastic reduction in Uganda's debt
burden indicators.32 As a result, external debt was US$1.5 billion (13 percent o f GDP) at
end-2006/07, compared with US$4.5 billion (47 percent o f GDP) a year earlier.
Consequently, all debt burden indicators are currently below their policy-dependent
thresholds.33 The debt service-to-exports ratio, the key indicator o f short-term external
liquidity,fell from 15.3 percent in2004/05 to 6.5 percent in2006/07.



31 Prepared by the IMF and World Bank staff. DSA assumptions and results have been discussed thoroughly
with the authorities. All debt indicators refer to Uganda's fiscal year (July-June).
32 Total MDRIrelief (including future interest) delivered in2005/06 and 2006/07 approached US$3.6 billion.
33 The World Bank's Country Policy and Institutional Assessment (CPIA) ranks Uganda as a "strong
performer." Debt burden thresholds for strong performers are NPV o f debt to GDP ratio o f 50 percent, NPV o f


                                                        104

2.        With lack of infrastructure being one of the key constraints to growth, the
authorities plan to increase spending on infrastructure. Infrastructure development (in
transportation, electricity, and water) has beengivenpriority inthe 2007/08 budget and inthe
medium term. The authorities plan to build the Bujagali hydroelectric plant to help ease
power constraints. The project i s being financed by a private consortium with participation
from multilateral lenders. However, as o f mid-November, the terms, conditions and
guarantees for financing had not been finalized. The construction o f one more hydroelectric
plant, possibly at Karuma, and of infrastructure necessary for the development of the oil
sector (such as a small refinery andpipelines) are still at the planningstage.

3.        The baseline DSA assesses the implicationsof the authorities' plans to increase
spending on infrastructure.The baseline DSA assumes that the government would contract
or guarantee debt on non-concessional terms up to US$400 million, with annual average
disbursements o f % percent o f GDP.34 The baseline scenario therefore shows the impact o f
partial public financing on non-concessional terms o f the investment in infrastructure. In
addition, the DSA assumes that multilateral and bilateral official debt would be contracted on
concessional terms. However, the baseline DSA excludes a number o f factors that are
difficult to assess and quantify at this stage, specifically: (i)the construction o f the Karuma
hydroelectric plant; (ii)the investment in infrastructure in the oil sector; (iii)and oil
production (expected to commence in 2009), as the scale o f production i s yet to be
determined. Box 1summarizes the key assumptions o fthe baseline DSA.




debt-to-exports ratio o f 200 percent, NPV o f debt-to-revenue ratio o f 300 percent, debt-service-to-exports ratio
o f 25 percent, and debt-service-to-revenue ratio of 35 percent.
34 The investment in the Bujagali project is expected to reach US$800million. Plans call for the investment to
be financed through equity participation in, and loans to the private consortium. The government intends to
negotiate favorable financing terms inthe event it ends up financing a portion o fthe project.


                                                        105

                            Box 1.Key AssumptionsUnderlyingthe BaselineDSA

Underthe baseline scenario, construction ofthe Bujagalihydroelectric plant begins in2007/08 andwillbe
completed by 2009/10.

Reflecting the higher investment ininfrastructure andthe subsequent increase inproduction, real GDP growth
would average 7 percent between 2007/08 and 2012113, before slowing to 6 percent by 2019/20.

Exports of goods are projected to grow 11% percent on average between 2007/08 and 2026/27, drivenlargely
byan increaseinthe export volume ofnon-traditional exports.

The current account deficit would be above its historical normof 5 percent of GDP by 2%percentage points
on average between 2007/08 and 2012113 (peaking at 9% percent o f GDP in 2008/09) on account o f higher
imports related to the construction o f the Bujagali plant. Ongoing adjustment o f the economy (reflected in a
growing share o f non-traditional exports) would help the current account deficit stabilize at 6 percent o f GDP in
the outer years. These trends would imply a gradual improvement in the current account deficit excluding
official transfers from 8% percent o f GDP in2006/07 to 7 percent o f GDP in2026/27.

Fiscal revenues are assumed to increase gradually from 13% percent o f GDP in 2006/07 to 16 -%percent o f
GDP in 2012/13. With grants declining below 3 percentage points o f GDP in the mediumterm, non-interest
expenditures are projected to decline slightly to about 19 percent o f GDP, consistent with a primary balance
close to zero inthe outer years.

Officialexternal loans are projectedto amount to US$485 millionper year on average throughout the medium
term, and US$420 million per year in the outer years. The DSA assumes that IDA will support Uganda with
lending operations throughout the projection period.

Compared with the 2006 DSA, the current baseline scenario assumes higher real GDP growth, higher imports
(particularly over the next three years, owing to imports for the construction o f the Bujagali plant), and higher
exports (reflecting the recent good performance and improved prospects for the sector). Export projections in
particular are driven by WE0 forecasts o f stronger growth o f Uganda's partner country demand for non-oil
imports, expected to average 8% percent inreal terms over 2007-13 and assumedto grow at the same rate up to
2027. These projections allow for small gains in export market shares over the mediumterm, and assume that
the strong export performance over the past four years will not be reversed. On balance, the new assumptions
result in a somewhat better current account. The current baseline scenario also includes an upward revision o f
the MDRI relief granted to Uganda (which now includes relief o f US$486 million from the AfDF not included
inthe 2006 DSA) and a downward revision to expected external loans inline with the authorities' projections.
The fiscal assumptionsremainbroadly unchanged.




                                                      106

                       I. EXTERNAL           DEBTSUSTAIN ABILITY ANALYSIS

(a) Baseline scenario

4.      The external debt dynamics during the next 20 years would be favorable
(Tables 1, la, and lb, and Figure 1).All three debt-burden indicators are expected to remain
well below their policy-dependent thresholds throughout the period. Reflecting the borrowing
for spending on infrastructure, the NPV o f debt-to-GDP ratio i s expected to rise from
5.9 percent in 2006/07 to 9.9 percent in 2012/13, but decline to 6.9 percent by 2026/27. The
NPV of debt-to-exports is expected to peak at 61.4 percent in 2009/10 and decline
substantially thereafter. The debt service-to-exports ratio is expected to continue along a
downward trend, reflecting the deliveryo fHIPC andMDRIassistance.

(b) Standardizedsensitivity analysis
5.      The baseline scenario points to low risk of debt distress. Under all the
standardized stress tests (see Table lb and Figure l),       the debt-to-GDP, debt-to-exports, and
debt service-to-exports indicators remain below their threshold values throughout the next
20 years.

6.      However, a large macroeconomic shock could worsen Uganda's NPV of debt-to-
exports ratio significantly. A combined shock (by one-half standard deviation) to growth,
exports, GDP deflator, and non-debt creating flows in 2007/08-2008/09, would raise
Uganda's NPV o f debt-to-exports ratio to 152.8 percent in2008/09. Such a shock would have
a significant impact on debt sustainability by putting the Ugandan economy at a high



                                                  107

 indebtedness level for a prolonged period. Uganda's NPV of the debt-to-GDP and debt-
 service ratios would nonetheless remainwell below the policy-dependent thresholds.

 (e) Customized sensitivity analysis-High   investment scenario
 7.      An alternative scenario has been developed to reflect even higher public
 investment in infrastructure. In addition to the investment envisaged in the baseline
 scenario, the alternative scenario includes investment inKaruma and the oil sector (a total o f
 US$422     million   during 2007/08-2010/1 l),and in other             infrastructure  projects
 (US$1.5 billion during the first decade, and US$2 billion during the subsequent decade).
 Public financing for these investments is assumed to be through a blend o f concessional (one
 quarter) and nonconcessional (three quarters) loans.

 8.     Under this scenario, debt ratios will remain within their policy dependent
 thresholds (Figure 2). Under all but one o f the standardized stress tests, the NPV o f debt-to-
 GDP and debt-to-exports as well as the debt service-to-exports ratio remain well below their
threshold values throughout the next 20 years. However, under the combined shock to
 growth, exports, GDP deflator, and non-debt creating flows (most extreme stress test), the
NPV of debt-to-exports threshold is breached in2008/09-2010/11. This result is sensitive to
the share o f non-concessional borrowing in total borrowing (75 percent). The smaller this
share i s the lower are Uganda's debt indicators, including under the combined stress tests.
Uganda could therefore become vulnerable to debt distress should it rely excessively on non-
concessional borrowing.

                        11.   FISCALDEBTSUSTAINABILITY        ANALYSIS

9.      The fiscal DSA is based on the assumption of continued fiscal consolidation,
though initially at a more moderate pace due to the power crisis, and on a gradual
tapering off of grant inflows. Specifically, it is assumed that emergency budget spendingon
the energy crisis will amount to about 2 percent o f GDP cumulatively inthe next two years,
and that grants will continue to decline from 6.3 percent of GDP in FY 2006/07, before
stabilizing at below 3 percent of GDP inthe medium term. Domestic revenues are projected
to increase gradually to some 16% percent o f GDP in 2012/13, in line with the authorities'
policy objective. After a spike related to the energy crisis, non-interest expenditures will
decline moderately as a percent of GDP, but then will increase again to about 19 percent o f
GDP, in line with projected improvement in domestic revenue collection, with primary
balance close to zero.

10.     Under the baseline scenario, the NPV of public debt is projected to increase
gradually after a sharp decline resulting from MDRI (Tables 2a, 2b, and Figure 3). It will
peak at about 19 percent of GDP in four years, and then decline gradually. Debt-service
indicatorsremain manageable, with debt-service not exceeding 10percent ofrevenues.




                                               108

11.     Uganda's publicdebtwill remainsustainablein case of shocks. Evenunder the
extreme stress test, the NPV of public debt will not exceed 30 percent of GDP over the
projection period. Similarly, the stress tests do not indicate any debt-servicing problem.

                                      111.    CONCLUSION

12.     The DSA analysis shows that Uganda's public debt remains sustainable under
the baselinescenario. Uganda's public debt has been reduced significantly as a result o f the
MDRI, andwith a prudentborrowing strategy and the continuation of the stability-oriented
fiscal policy, debt should remain comfortably low during the projection period. While the
alternative scenario shows that Uganda can adopt a higher investment program, caution on
borrowing i s warranted and reliance on concessional financing remains essential.
